Talaq — Divorce
beginner
6 min
Conditions for Valid Suspended Divorce (Ta'liq al-Talaq)
Explains condition of ownership for ta'liq al-talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions for a valid suspended divorce in the Hanafi school and apply them to determine whether a suspended divorce is binding.

Core Idea
A suspended divorce is valid only if the husband owns the wife at the time of the condition.

In this lesson, we examine the conditions for a valid suspended divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) as laid out in the Hanafi text. The central condition is ownership (milk) over the wife at the moment the condition is uttered. The passage also lists three other conditions: the condition must be uncertain, connected without a significant gap, and explicitly mentioned. We will parse each condition and apply them to concrete scenarios to understand when a suspended divorce takes effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chapter context
The passage begins by stating the primary condition for validity of suspended divorce: ownership or addition to ownership. Recognize that this chapter comes after immediate divorce (tanjiz) and delegation (tafwid).
2
List the four conditions
From the commentary, extract the four conditions: (1) the condition must be non-existent and uncertain (mumkin mutaraddad), (2) connected to the consequence without a significant interval, (3) explicitly mentioned, (4) ownership of the wife or addition to future ownership.
The first three are common to ta'liq; the fourth is unique to divorce/emancipation.
3
Apply the ownership condition
Use the example in the text: a man says to a non-wife (ajnabiyyah) 'If I speak to you, you are divorced.' Later he marries her and speaks. The divorce does not occur because he did not own her at the time of the condition.
Contrast with a statement to a wife: ownership exists, so condition triggers divorce.
4
Understand the exception
If the husband adds the suspension to future ownership (e.g., 'If I marry you, you are divorced'), the divorce becomes effective upon marriage because the condition is tied to the acquisition of ownership.
This is the meaning of 'addition to ownership' (al-idafa ilayh).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحلف بالطلاق (شرط صحته" الملك ، أو الإضافة إليه (١) ، فلا تطلق أجنبية قال لها : إن كلمتك فأنت كذا، فنكحها فكلّمها. [١] قوله : باب الحلف بالطلاق ; لَمَّا فرغ عن صور تنجيز الطلاق وتفويضه، شرع في مسائل تعليق الطلاق، والتعليقُ عبارة عن ربط حصول مضمون جملة بحصول جملة أخرى، ويُسمَّى هذا الربط في عرفِ الفقهاء وغيرهم يميناً وحلفاً ؛ لأن اليمين في الأصل القوّة، وفي التعليق قوّة، فاليمينُ والحلف بالطلاق أو العتاق يراد به تعليقه على أمر، وتفصيله ليطلب من «البحر» و«النهر» و«الفتح» وغيرها. [٢] قوله : شرط صحته ... الخ ; اعلم أنه يشترط له أمور: ا منها : أن يكون الشرط معدوماً متردّداً بين أن يكون وبين أن لا يكون، نحو: إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ، فإن كان محققاً كإن كانت السماء فوقنا، فهو تنجيز، وإن كان مستحيلاً فهو لغو، كإن وجد شريك الباري. ٢. ومنها : كون الشطر متصلاً بالجزاء بلا فاصل يعتد به. ٣. ومنها : ذكر الشرط، ففي أنت طالق أن يكون لغواً. ٤ . ومنها : أن يوجد (٤) الملك أو الإضافة إليه. [٣] قوله : فلا تطلق ; يعني إذا قال لأجنبية : إن كلمتك فأنت طالق ، أو إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ، ثمّ تزوّجها ووجد الشرط بعده لا يقع الطلاق بالتعليق السابق ؛ لأنه لم يكن يملكها عند ذلك، ولا أضافه إلى الملك، بخلاف ما إذا قال لزوجته ، فإنّه يُنزل الجزاء بوجود الشرط.
Chapter of Swearing by Divorce (Its condition for validity is ownership or addition to it), so a foreign woman (non-wife) is not divorced if he said to her: 'If I speak to you, then you are such and such', then he marries her and speaks to her. [1] His statement: 'Chapter of Swearing by Divorce' — when he finished from the forms of immediate divorce and delegation, he began in the issues of suspended divorce (ta'liq). Ta'liq is an expression for linking the occurrence of the content of one sentence to the occurrence of another sentence. This linking is called in the terminology of jurists and others 'yamin' and 'half' because yamin originally means strength, and in suspension there is strength. So yamin and half by divorce or manumission intend the suspension upon a matter. Its detail is sought from 'al-Bahr', 'al-Nahr', 'al-Fath' and others. [2] His statement: 'Its condition for validity...' etc. Know that it has several conditions: (1) The condition must be non-existent, fluctuating between existence and non-existence, such as 'if you enter the house, you are divorced'. If it is certain (like 'if the sky is above us'), it is immediate (tanjiz). If it is impossible, it is meaningless (laghw), such as 'if a partner of Allah exists'. (2) The condition must be connected to the consequence without a significant interval. (3) Mentioning the condition; if not mentioned, it is meaningless. (4) The existence of ownership or addition to it. [3] His statement: 'so a foreign woman is not divorced' means that if he says to a foreign woman: 'If I speak to you, you are divorced', or 'If you enter the house, you are divorced', then he marries her and the condition occurs afterwards, the divorce does not occur by the prior suspension because he did not own her at that time, nor did he add it to ownership. Contrary to if he said it to his wife, then the consequence (divorce) follows upon occurrence of the condition.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on suspended divorce (ta'liq al-talaq). It begins by stating the primary condition for validity: the husband must have ownership (milk) over the wife at the time of the suspension, or add the suspension to future ownership (al-idafa ilayh) such as saying 'if I marry you, you are divorced'. The ruling is illustrated with an example: if a man says to a non-wife (ajnabiyya) 'If I speak to you, you are divorced', then later marries her and speaks to her, the divorce does not occur because at the time of the condition he did not own her. The commentary then details additional conditions for a valid suspended divorce: the condition must be uncertain (not certain or impossible), it must be connected without a significant gap, the condition must be explicitly mentioned, and ownership or addition to ownership must be present. These are the foundational rules of ta'liq in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
تعليق الطلاق (ta'liq al-talaq): Suspending the occurrence of divorce upon the occurrence of a future condition.
الملك (al-milk): Ownership of the wife, i.e., being in a valid marriage.
الإضافة إلى الملك (al-idafa ila al-milk): Adding the suspension to future ownership, e.g., saying 'if I marry you, you are divorced'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man tells a non-marriageable woman 'If I speak to you, you are divorced', then later marries her and speaks to her, what happens?
A) She is divorced immediately upon marriage
B) She is divorced when he speaks
C) No divorce occurs
D) The marriage is invalid

Key Takeaway
For a suspended divorce to be binding, the husband must own the wife at the time of the condition or explicitly add the suspension to future ownership.

Conditions for Valid Suspension of Divorce: The Hanafi Position and the Shafi'i Dispute
Conditions for valid ta'liq and Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for valid ta'liq in the Hanafi school and explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i objection.

Core Idea
Suspending divorce before marriage is valid if linked to future ownership – at condition fulfillment, ownership exists.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses the chapter on swearing by divorce. It clarifies when a conditional divorce (ta'liq) is valid under Hanafi law, focusing on the requirement of ownership at the time of suspension or an addition to future ownership. The commentator then presents the Shafi'i counterargument and the Hanafi rebuttal, providing key distinctions and textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the two conditions for valid ta'liq: ownership at the time of suspension (milk) or addition to future ownership (al-idafah ila al-milk). Note the examples: suspension on a current wife (valid) and suspension on an unrelated woman linked to marriage (valid).
2
Identify the Opposing View and its Basis
State that Imam al-Shafi'i holds such suspensions invalid because they lack ownership at the time of utterance. Understand his analogy: ta'liq is like immediate pronouncement (tanjiz), which requires current ownership.
3
Examine the Hanafi Proof (Dalil)
Review the Hanafi evidence: the practice of Ibn 'Umar narrated in al-Muwatta' where he gave a fatwa that a conditional divorce linked to future marriage occurs. Also the hadith 'La talaqa fima la tamlik' is interpreted by Hanafis as referring to immediate pronouncement, not suspension.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response to the Shafi'i Objection
Understand the key distinction: divorce occurs at the moment the condition is fulfilled, not at the time of suspension. At fulfillment, ownership exists. This differs from saying 'If you enter the house' to an unrelated woman, where ownership never arises.
The Hanafi response highlights that ta'liq is contingent; the effective moment is when the condition materializes.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
The practical difference: Under Hanafi law, if a man tells an unrelated woman 'If I marry you, you are divorced' and then marries her, divorce occurs. Under Shafi'i law, no divorce occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحلف بالطلاق
شرط صحته الملك، أو الإضافة إليه، فلا تطلق أجنبية قال لها: إن كلمتك فأنت كذا، فنكحها فكلمها.
وتطلق بعد الشرط إن قال لزوجته فكلمها أو قال لأجنبية: إن نكحتك فأنت كذا فنكحها.
وعند الشافعي لا يقع.
قوله: إن نكحتك؛ وكذا إن نكحت امرأة، وكذا إذا قال: إن نكحت فلانة فهي طالق واحدة أو اثنتين أو ثلاثاً، فيقع بعد النكاح ما علق به، كذا أفتى به ابن عمر أخرجه مالك في «الموطأ».
قوله: لا يقع؛ لأنّ التعليق كالتنجيز، فكما لا يمكن التنجيز في حال عدم الملك، كذلك لا يصح التعليق. وجوابه: إنّ التعليق بقوله: إن نكحتك فأنت طالق، وإن وجد في الحال لكن الطلاق يوجد عند وجود الشرط، وعند ذلك يتحقق الملك المجوز للطلاق، بخلاف قوله لأجنبية: إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق، فإنّه لا أثر للملك هناك لا حالاً ولا مآلاً فلا يقع الطلاق به، كما لا يقع الطلاق المنجز على الأجنبية. وعلى هذا يحمل قوله: «لا طلاق فيما لا يملك»، أخرجه الترمذي وحسنه، وابن ماجة، وعند ابن ماجة مرفوعاً: لا طلاق قبل النكاح.
Chapter on Swearing by Divorce. The condition of its validity is ownership, or addition to it. So an unrelated woman does not become divorced if he said to her: 'If I speak to you, then you are such-and-such,' then he marries her and speaks to her. And she becomes divorced after the condition if he said to his wife and then she spoke, or if he said to an unrelated woman: 'If I marry you, then you are such-and-such,' and then he marries her. And according to al-Shafi'i, it does not occur. His statement: 'If I marry you'; and likewise if he said: 'If I marry a woman,' and likewise if he said: 'If I marry so-and-so, she is divorced once, twice, or three times,' then after the marriage what he suspended it upon occurs. This is what Ibn 'Umar gave a fatwa on, recorded by Malik in al-Muwatta'. His statement: 'It does not occur' because suspension is like immediate pronouncement; just as immediate pronouncement is not possible in the state of absence of ownership, likewise suspension is not valid. Its answer: Suspension by his saying 'If I marry you, you are divorced' exists at that moment, but the divorce occurs at the occurrence of the condition, and at that time ownership permitting divorce is realized, unlike his saying to an unrelated woman: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' for there is no effect of ownership there neither immediately nor eventually, so divorce does not occur by it, just as immediate divorce does not occur on an unrelated woman. And upon this is carried his statement: 'There is no divorce in what one does not own' reported by al-Tirmidhi and he declared it good, and Ibn Majah, and according to Ibn Majah as marfu': 'There is no divorce before marriage.'
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the chapter on swearing by divorce (al-hilf bi al-talaq). The commentator first clarifies the condition for the validity of suspending divorce: the husband must own the wife at the time of making the suspension (ta'liq), or he must add the suspension to future ownership (e.g., 'If I marry you, you are divorced'). If he suspends divorce on a condition involving an unrelated woman (ajnabiyyah) without linking it to future ownership, such as 'If I speak to you, you are divorced,' and later marries her and speaks to her, the divorce does not occur because at the time of suspension he did not own her and did not link it to ownership. In contrast, if he says to his wife 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' and she enters, divorce occurs because ownership existed at the time of suspension. Similarly, if he says to an unrelated woman 'If I marry you, you are divorced' and then marries her, divorce occurs because the suspension is added to future ownership (al-idafah ila al-milk). The Hanafi position is that the condition is valid and divorce occurs upon fulfillment of the condition. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that suspension is like immediate pronouncement: just as immediate divorce cannot occur without ownership, suspension is invalid without current ownership. The Hanafi response is that divorce occurs at the moment the condition is fulfilled, at which point ownership exists. This is distinguished from the case of 'If you enter the house' said to an ajnabiyyah, where ownership never occurs. The hadith 'La talaqa fima la tamlik' (No divorce in what you do not own) is interpreted by Hanafis as referring to immediate divorce before marriage, not to valid suspensions.

Key Terms
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Linking the occurrence of a divorce to the occurrence of a future condition.
الإضافة إلى الملك (al-idafah ila al-milk): Linking the suspended divorce to the future acquisition of ownership, e.g., 'If I marry you'.
التنجيز (al-tanjiz): Immediate pronouncement of divorce without any condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to an unrelated woman: 'If I marry you, you are divorced.' He then marries her. According to the Hanafi school, what happens?
A) The divorce occurs upon marriage.
B) The divorce does not occur because he did not own her at the time of the statement.
C) The divorce occurs only if he repeats the statement after marriage.
D) The marriage is invalid because of the suspended divorce.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a suspended divorce linked to future ownership because at the moment of condition fulfillment, ownership exists, distinguishing it from immediate pronouncement.

Conditional Divorce on Wife vs. Stranger and the Shafi'i Dispute
Conditional divorce on wife versus stranger with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates conditional divorce on a stranger and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the key concepts of ta'liq, tanziz, and milk.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce takes effect only when ownership exists—the timing of the condition, not the utterance, determines validity.

This passage addresses a classic inter-madhhab dispute: Is a divorce conditional upon marriage valid when pronounced before marriage? The Hanafi school affirms it, while Imam al-Shafi'i denies it. By examining the arguments, you will see how the concepts of suspension (ta'liq) versus immediate execution (tanziz) and the role of ownership (milk) shape the ruling. The lesson will equip you to distinguish between valid and invalid conditional divorces and appreciate how hadith interpretations differ.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from the matn: if a man says to a stranger 'if I marry you, you are divorced' and then marries her, divorce occurs upon marriage.
The key phrase is 'إضافة إلى الملك' (addition to ownership).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that such conditional divorce is invalid, equating ta'liq with tanziz.
Al-Shafi'i's position is introduced as 'لا يقع' (it does not occur).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Al-Shafi'i argues that ta'liq is like tanziz; just as immediate divorce on a non-wife is invalid, so is conditional divorce. He cites the hadith 'no divorce in what one does not own.'
The hadith is reported by Tirmidhi, Ibn Majah, and al-Hakim.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes between the time of utterance and the time of effect: divorce occurs only when ownership is realized (at marriage). Unlike a pure conditional statement on a stranger that never brings ownership (e.g., 'if you enter the house'), here ownership arises at the condition's fulfillment.
The hadith is reinterpreted to refer to immediate divorce on another's wife, not conditional divorce.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: In Hanafi law, the marriage is valid but divorce immediately takes effect. In Shafi'i law, the marriage is valid and no divorce occurs.
This difference affects real-world fatwa and court rulings in Hanafi-majority regions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتطلق بعد الشرط إن قال لزوجته فكلّمها أو قال لأجنبية : إن نكحتك فأنت كذا فنكحها وتطلق بعد الشرط ) إن قال لزوجته فكلّمها )؛ لوجود الملك وقت التعليق، (أو قال لأجنبية : إن نكحتك ) فأنت كذا فنكحها؛ لوجود الإضافة إلى الملك، (١) قوله : إن نكحتك ؛ وكذا إن نكحت امرأة، وكذا إذا قال : إن نكحت فلانة فهي طالق واحدة أو اثنتين أو ثلاثاً، فيقع بعد النكاح ما علق به ، كذا أفتى به ابن عمر أخرجه مالك في «الموطأ» (۳). قوله : لا يقع ؛ لأنّ التعليق كالتنجيز ، فكما لا يمكن التنجيزُ في حال عدم الملك، كذلك لا يصح التعليق. وجوابه : إنّ التعليق بقوله : إن نكحتك فأنت طالق ، وإن وجد في الحال لكن الطلاق يوجد عند وجودِ الشرط ، وعند ذلك يتحقق الملك المجوز للطلاق، بخلاف قوله لأجنبية : إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ، فإنّه لا أثر للملك هناك لا حالاً ولا مآلاً فلا يقعُ الطلاق به، كما لا يقع الطلاق المنجز على الأجنبية. وعلى هذا يحمل قوله : « لا طلاق فيما لا يملك»)، أخرجه الترمذي وحسنه، وابن ماجة، وعند ابن ماجة مرفوعاً: لا طلاق قبل النكاح)، وعند الحاكم : «لا طلاق إلا بعد نكاح (۲) ، فاستدلال الشافعي به لا يصح. وقد روى عبد الرزاق عن معمر عن الزهري الله أنه قال في رجل قال : «كل امرأة أتزوّجها فهي طالق وكلّ أمةٍ اشتريتها فهي حرّة، هو كما قال ، فقال له معمر: أو ليس جاء : لا طلاق قبل نكاح ولا عتق إلا بعد «ملك»، قال: إنما ذلك أن يقول الرجل : امرأة فلان طالق ، وعبد فلان حر» (۳). وإن شئت زيادة التفصيل في هذه المسألة فارجع إلى تعليقي المتعلق بـ«موطأ محمد» المسمّى بـ «التعليق الممجد».
And divorce occurs after the condition if he says to his wife 'if you speak' or he says to a stranger 'if I marry you, you are such-and-such' then he marries her, and divorce occurs after the condition if he says to his wife 'if you speak' because of the existence of ownership at the time of suspension, or if he says to a stranger 'if I marry you' then you are such-and-such then he marries her because of the addition to ownership. (1) His statement: 'if I marry you' and likewise 'if I marry a woman' and likewise if he says 'if I marry so-and-so she is divorced with one, two, or three divorces, then after marriage what was suspended occurs. Thus Ibn Umar issued a fatwa, reported by Malik in al-Muwatta. (2) His statement: 'it does not occur' because suspension is like immediate execution, so just as immediate execution cannot occur in the absence of ownership, similarly suspension is not valid. And its answer: That suspension by his statement 'if I marry you, you are divorced' although it occurs in the present, but the divorce occurs at the occurrence of the condition, and at that time ownership that permits divorce is realized, unlike his statement to a stranger 'if you enter the house, you are divorced' for there is no effect of ownership there neither at present nor in the future, so divorce does not occur by it, just as immediate divorce does not occur on a stranger. And upon this is carried his statement 'no divorce in what one does not own' reported by Tirmidhi and he declared it hasan, and by Ibn Majah, and according to Ibn Majah as marfu': 'no divorce before marriage', and according to al-Hakim: 'no divorce except after marriage', so al-Shafi'i's deduction by it is not valid. And Abd al-Razzaq narrated from Ma'mar from al-Zuhri that he said concerning a man who said: 'every woman I marry is divorced and every slave I buy is free, it is as he said.' So Ma'mar said: 'Has it not come: no divorce before marriage and no emancipation except after ownership?' He said: 'That is only when a man says: so-and-so's wife is divorced and so-and-so's slave is free.' And if you wish additional detail on this issue, refer to my commentary on Muwatta Muhammad titled al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on the validity of conditional divorce pronounced upon a stranger before marriage. The Hanafi position, stated in the matn, is that if a man says to his wife 'if you speak, you are divorced,' the divorce occurs when she speaks because ownership existed at the time of suspension. If he says to a stranger 'if I marry you, you are divorced,' and then marries her, the divorce occurs upon marriage because the suspension was added to ownership (i.e., conditional upon marriage). Ibn Umar's fatwa in the Muwatta supports this. Al-Shafi'i objects, arguing that suspension (ta'liq) is like immediate execution (tanziz); as immediate divorce on a non-wife is impossible, so is suspension. The Hanafi response clarifies that while the statement is made before marriage, the divorce itself only takes effect at the moment the condition is fulfilled, at which point ownership exists. This differs from saying to a stranger 'if you enter the house, you are divorced,' where ownership never enters, so no divorce occurs. The hadith 'no divorce in what one does not own' is interpreted to refer to divorcing another man's wife, not to conditional divorce upon marriage. The narration from al-Zuhri explicitly distinguishes between the two cases. Thus, the Shafi'i reliance on the hadith is incorrect.

Key Terms
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a legal consequence (like divorce) upon the occurrence of a future condition.
تنجيز (tanziz): Immediate execution of a legal act, like pronouncing divorce on a present wife.
ملك (milk): Ownership or right over a spouse that permits divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school consider conditional divorce valid when a man says to a stranger 'if I marry you, you are divorced'?
A) Because the condition itself creates ownership immediately.
B) Because ownership for divorce is realized at the moment of condition fulfillment, not at the time of pronouncing the condition.
C) Because the stranger has consented to the condition.
D) Because the hadith 'no divorce in what one does not own' is inauthentic.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a conditional divorce on a stranger is valid because divorce takes effect only when ownership is realized at the condition's fulfillment, distinguishing it from invalid immediate divorce on a non-owned person.

Particles of Condition in Divorce Oaths
List and explanation of condition particles in divorce oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the Hanafi condition particles for divorce oaths and explain why 'kull' and 'kullama' are legally treated as such despite grammatical objections.

Core Idea
Condition particles in divorce oaths include 'kull' and 'kullama' because the verb is implied.

In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of a conditional divorce depends on the words used to express the condition. This passage from ‘Umdat al-Ri‘aya enumerates the standard particles (alfaz al-shart) and explains why even grammatically irregular terms like 'kull' are accepted. Understanding these particles is essential for drafting or interpreting divorce oaths correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard list
Scan the Arabic text and list all the particles mentioned: 'in', 'idha', 'idhama', 'kull', 'kullama', 'mata', 'matama'. Note that the list is not exhaustive.
The author deliberately uses 'words' to include both letters and nouns.
2
Understand the derivation of 'shart'
The commentary explains that 'shart' comes from 'sharat' meaning 'sign'. In legal theory, a condition is a sign that indicates the consequence (jazaa) will occur. This etymological basis helps explain why certain words are accepted.
Condition is not just a grammatical category but a legal indicator.
3
Analyze the inclusion of 'kull' and 'kullama'
Grammarians object because condition particles are normally followed by a verb, but 'kull' is followed by a noun. The Hanafi response: the verb is implicitly attached to the noun (e.g., 'every slave I buy' implies 'if I buy a slave'). Thus, legally, it functions as a condition.
This shows fiqh prioritizes legal effect over grammatical strictness.
4
Recognize the legal nuance: 'addition to ownership'
The phrase 'bi-al-idafati ila al-mulk' means the divorce is suspended upon a condition related to ownership of the wife (i.e., marriage). This distinguishes valid conditional divorce from impossible or ambiguous conditions.
The condition must be about something within the husband's control or a future event.

Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a definition and enumeration of the particles of condition (alfaz al-shart) used in divorce oaths. The matn lists 'in', 'idha', 'idhama', 'kull' (with example 'every woman of mine who enters the house is divorced'), 'kullama', 'mata', and 'matama'. The commentator explains that the phrase 'addition to ownership' means that the divorce is suspended upon the occurrence of ownership (i.e., marriage). The commentary then clarifies that the intended meaning of 'addition' is not linguistic but legal: divorce is linked to a sign of ownership. The choice of the term 'words' rather than 'letters' or 'nouns' is deliberate to include 'in' which is a letter (harf) and others which are nouns. The word 'shart' itself derives from 'sharat' meaning 'sign' because it signals the existence of the consequence. The list is not exhaustive; many other words like 'law', 'ayna', etc., are also used. Finally, the commentator notes that grammarians do not consider 'kull' and 'kullama' as true condition particles because condition particles are typically followed by a verb, while 'kull' is followed by a noun. However, in legal usage, 'kull' is treated as a condition because the verb is implicitly related to the noun it modifies, as in 'every slave I buy is free'.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A condition upon which a legal consequence (like divorce) is suspended; etymologically, a sign.
ألفاظ الشرط (alfaz al-shart): Words used to express a condition in legal oaths, including 'in', 'idha', etc.
بالإضافة إلى الملك (bi-al-idafati ila al-mulk): The legal addition or connection of a condition to ownership, meaning the divorce is conditioned on acquiring ownership.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi fiqh expands the list of condition particles beyond grammatical norms to include 'kull' and 'kullama' based on legal logic, ensuring that divorce oaths are given effect when the intended condition is clear.

Conditional Divorce Words and the Hadith 'No Divorce Without Marriage'
Defines conditional talaq words and refutes Shafi'i objection via al-Zuhri's interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school upholds conditional divorce prior to marriage and evaluate the Shafi'i objection based on the hadith.

Core Idea
Conditional divorce before marriage is valid; the hadith only bars direct, non-conditional declarations.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya defines the conditional words used in talaq and addresses a key inter-madhhab dispute: the validity of a conditional divorce pronounced before marriage. The Shafi'i school invokes the hadith 'No divorce except after marriage' to reject such conditions, but the Hanafi response, grounded in a report from Imam al-Zuhri, restricts the hadith's application and upholds the conditional pronouncement. Understanding this dispute reveals how Hanafi usul principles and textual interpretation operate.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: conditional talaq (e.g., 'Every woman I marry is divorced') is valid and becomes effective upon marriage. The condition is linked to future ownership (al-idafatu ila al-mulk).
The list of conditional words includes 'in', 'idha', 'kull', 'kullama', etc.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection and their dalil
Note that al-Shafi'i uses the hadith 'la talaqa illa ba'da nikah' (no divorce except after marriage) to argue that any divorce pronouncement before marriage is invalid, whether conditional or not.
The hadith is narrated by al-Hakim and others.
3
Examine the Hanafi response via al-Zuhri
The Hanafi response cites a report from Abd al-Razzaq from Ma'mar from al-Zuhri. Al-Zuhri distinguishes: the hadith applies only when one says 'So-and-so's wife is divorced' without any ownership; it does not apply to conditional statements like 'Every woman I marry is divorced'.
This restricts the general wording of the hadith to a specific context.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under Hanafi law, if a man says 'Every woman I marry is divorced' and then marries, the wife is divorced immediately upon marriage. Under Shafi'i law, such a condition is void and the marriage proceeds without divorce.
The practical consequence affects marital rights and obligations.
5
Confirm the mufta bihi (relied-upon position)
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi, as supported by the author of 'Umda al-Ri'aya who cites al-Zuhri's interpretation and dismisses the Shafi'i inference as invalid.
The author also refers readers to his own commentary 'al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad' for more detail.

Commentary
This passage is a combined matn and sharh on the definition of conditional words (adawat al-shart) used in talaq and a refutation of a Shafi'i objection. The matn lists the common conditional words: in, idha, idhama, kull, kullama, mata, mata ma. It then clarifies that 'idafa ila al-mulk' means linking divorce to ownership (i.e., making divorce conditional upon ownership), not the literal or customary addition. The commentary then addresses a hadith reported by al-Hakim: 'No divorce except after marriage' which the Shafi'i school uses to argue that conditional divorce before marriage is invalid. The Hanafi response, via a report from al-Zuhri, explains that the hadith applies only when one says 'So-and-so's wife is divorced' without any ownership, not to general conditionals like 'Every woman I marry is divorced.' Thus, conditional divorce is valid. The commentary further explains that the list is not exhaustive, and includes grammatical notes on why 'kull' and 'kullama' are considered conditional words in fiqh despite not being so in grammar. The key takeaway is that Hanafis uphold the validity of conditional talaq and interpret the hadith narrowly.

Key Terms
الإضافة إلى الملك (al-idafatu ila al-mulk): Linking divorce to the ownership of the wife, i.e., making divorce conditional upon the occurrence of ownership (like marriage). Not literal addition.
ألفاظ الشرط (alfaz al-shart): Words that indicate a condition, such as 'in', 'idha', 'kullama', etc. In this context, they are used to suspend talaq upon the occurrence of a future event.

Key Takeaway
Conditional divorce pronounced before marriage is valid in Hanafi fiqh because the hadith restricting divorce to after marriage is interpreted to apply only to direct, non-conditional declarations, not to conditions tied to future ownership.

Dissolution of the Oath in Conditional Divorce with 'Kullama'
Explains when the conditional oath dissolves and the exception for 'kullama' and conditions attached to marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the effect of 'kullama' from other conditional words in suspended divorce and explain when the oath dissolves or repeats.

Core Idea
With 'kullama', divorce repeats until three; with other words, the oath dissolves after one occurrence.

You already know that a suspended divorce can be conditional on an action. The words used matter. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies precisely when the conditional oath dissolves and when it does not. We will see the critical difference between 'kullama' and other particles, and an additional nuance when the condition is tied to marriage itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
For most conditional words (e.g., 'in', 'idha'), the oath (ta'liq) dissolves upon the first occurrence of the condition. The suspended divorce occurs only once.
This is the default Hanafi position.
2
Recognize the exception for 'kullama'
'Kullama' ('whenever') implies repetition (takrar). Each time the condition occurs, a divorce is triggered until three divorces are completed.
After three divorces, the marriage ends; further occurrences have no effect.
3
Understand the rationale
The oath is tied to the current marriage ownership. Once three divorces occur, the ownership ends, and the oath cannot apply to a new marriage (unless the condition is attached to marriage itself).
This is the 'inhlal al-yamin' — dissolution of the oath.
4
Consider the further exception
If the conditional phrase is attached to the cause of marriage (e.g., 'kullama tazawwajtuki fa-anti taliq'), the repetition continues with each new marriage, because the condition is linked to the act of marriage, not a specific ownership.
This exception prevents the oath from dissolving permanently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق/ الحلف بالطلاق والاستثناء

Book of Divorce / Oath by Divorce and Exception. So in these (words), the oath dissolves when the condition occurs once, except with 'kullama', for it dissolves after three (divorces), so it does not occur if he marries her after another husband, except if it is entered upon marriage, so in it the oath dissolves when the condition occurs once, except with 'kullama', for it dissolves after three. What is meant by dissolution of the oath: nullification of the oath by nullification of the suspension, so it does not occur if he marries her after another husband, except if it is entered upon marriage. [1] His saying: 'So in these' means if he suspends divorce by uttering these words, and the condition occurs, the suspension dissolves, and it remains only with 'kullama' which indicates repetition, like 'whenever you enter the house you are divorced', for she is divorced with every entry until three are completed. [2] His saying: 'So it does not occur' is a branching from his saying: 'for it dissolves after three'; that is because the thing sworn by is the divorce of this ownership, and it has ended with three, so its effect does not remain in a new ownership. [3] His saying: 'Except if ... etc.' is an exception from his saying: 'So it does not occur'; its summary: this ruling applies when it is not entered upon marriage, but if it is entered upon the cause of ownership such as his saying: 'Whenever I marry you, you are divorced', then it causes divorce to occur after every marriage, so divorce occurs upon her after every marriage.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with commentary on the effect of conditional words on the dissolution of the oath (inhlal al-yamin) in talaq. The matn states the general rule: with most conditional words (like 'in', 'idha', etc.), the oath dissolves upon the first occurrence of the condition, and the suspended divorce does not recur. The exception is 'kullama', which implies repetition (takrar); here, the divorce occurs with each occurrence of the condition until the three divorces are exhausted (after the third, the marriage is irretrievably dissolved). The commentary explains the rationale: the oath is tied to the ownership of the current marriage; once three divorces occur, the ownership ends, so the oath no longer applies to a new marriage. Another exception is when the conditional word is attached to the cause of ownership (sabab al-milk) itself, such as 'every time I marry you, you are divorced.' In that case, even after three divorces and remarriage to another man, the condition is considered renewed with each new marriage, and divorce occurs after every marriage. This is because the condition is linked to the act of marriage itself, not to a specific ownership.

Key Terms
انحلال اليمين (inhlal al-yamin): Nullification of the oath (yamin) by voidance of the suspension (ta'liq); the oath no longer applies after the condition is met or after the maximum effect.
كلما (kullama): A conditional word indicating repetition ('whenever'); used here to mean that the condition triggers the consequence each time it occurs, until the limit (three divorces) is reached.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) She is divorced each time she enters the house until three divorces occur
C) She is not divorced because the condition is invalid
D) She is divorced only if she enters the house before the marriage

Key Takeaway
With 'kullama', conditional divorce repeats until three; with other words, the oath dissolves after one occurrence; if attached to marriage, the repetition continues with each new marriage.

Talaq — Divorce

Conditional Divorce and the Effect of Marriage Dissolution
Commentary on conditional divorce rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce when the marriage is dissolved and apply the legal stratagem (hilah) to avoid triple divorce.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce oath does not dissolve when marriage ends; only the condition's occurrence dissolves it, but divorce requires the wife to be a valid subject.

This lesson examines a key commentary from Al-Wiqayah explaining the effect of dissolution of marriage on conditional divorce oaths. You will learn when the oath is nullified and when divorce actually occurs, as well as a legal stratagem used in the Hanafi school to avoid triple divorce. The passage clarifies the principle that the oath remains binding even after irrevocable divorce and only dissolves upon the actual occurrence of the condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The commentary states that dissolution of marriage (zawal al-milk) does not nullify the oath; the oath remains valid until the condition occurs.
2
Distinguish dissolution of oath from occurrence of divorce
When the condition occurs while the wife is still owned (in marriage or iddah), the oath dissolves and divorce takes effect. When it occurs after ownership ends, the oath dissolves but no divorce follows.
This distinction hinges on the presence of mahalliyyah (legal capacity to be divorced).
3
Understand the condition of mahalliyyah
For divorce to occur, the wife must be a valid subject for divorce at the time the condition is met. This requires an existing marriage or a legal iddah period.
Even if the oath remains, divorce cannot occur without mahalliyyah.
4
Apply the legal stratagem (hilah)
If a husband suspended three divorces on a condition, he can dissolve the oath by divorcing her once, letting the iddah expire, then she enters the condition while not married to him. This dissolves the oath without triggering triple divorce.
This hilah works only if the oath does not contain a word indicating repetition (e.g., kullama).
5
Resolve disputes about condition occurrence
If spouses differ on whether the condition occurred, the husband's word (with oath) is taken because he is the denier of the divorce's occurrence.
The principle that the denier's statement is preferred applies here.

For example: "Whenever I marry you, you are like this" - he breaks the oath every time, even after another husband. Because every time he marries her, she is divorced, even if it is after another husband. Dissolution of marriage does not nullify the oath, and it dissolves after the condition absolutely, and ownership is required for divorce. So his saying "absolutely" means whether the condition occurs during ownership or not. If it occurs during ownership, it dissolves to a penalty, i.e. the oath is nullified and the penalty follows. His saying: "Dissolution of marriage does not nullify the oath" etc. means that if he suspends divorce on a condition like "If you enter the house, you are divorced," then he removes her from his ownership by irrevocably divorcing her, the oath is not nullified but remains. So if he marries her and the condition occurs, she is divorced. Similarly, emancipation and other things. His saying: "and it dissolves" means the oath is nullified and the suspension is dissolved by the occurrence of the condition absolutely. If the condition occurs during his ownership, the oath is dissolved and the penalty follows, so divorce occurs. If it occurs outside his ownership, such as after the suspension he separates her irrevocably, then the condition occurs, the oath is dissolved, and divorce does not occur due to the lack of the capacity to be divorced. So if he remarries her and the condition occurs, divorce does not occur because the oath dissolved before that. His saying: "If it occurs..." etc. The summary is that the dissolution of the oath occurs by the condition once if there is no word in his oath indicating repetition. The meaning of its dissolution is the nullification of the suspension such that no effect remains thereafter, so even if the condition occurs afterwards, the penalty does not follow. Then, it is either that the condition occurs while the marriage ownership remains or after its removal. If the first, the penalty follows, i.e., divorce occurs due to the existence of capacity to be divorced. If the second, divorce does not occur, and no effect of the suspension remains thereafter in both cases. His saying: "the penalty" is divorce in our context. Similarly, the ruling applies to emancipation and similar. So if he says: "If you enter the house, you are free," then he enters the house while his ownership stands, he becomes free. If he sold him after the suspension, then he enters the house, the oath is nullified without emancipation following, so even if he buys him afterwards and he enters the house, he does not become free. This means that if the marriage ends and the condition has not occurred, the oath is not nullified. The nullification of the oath occurs by the occurrence of the condition whether in ownership or its removal, but the occurrence of divorce requires the oath. And if it occurs outside ownership, it dissolves without a penalty, i.e., the oath is nullified and no penalty follows due to the lack of capacity to be divorced. So if he says: "If you enter the house, you are divorced three times," and he intends that she enter the house without the three divorces occurring, his stratagem is to divorce her once, and the waiting period ends, then she enters the house so that the oath is nullified and the three do not occur. Then he marries her. If she enters the house, nothing occurs due to the nullification of the oath. (And if they differ on the occurrence of the condition, the husband's word is taken.) [1] His saying: "due to the lack of capacity" means that she is not capable of being divorced because the condition for divorce is the existence of marriage ownership. [2] His saying: "intends" means he intends, after suspending the occurrence of three on her entering the house, that it not occur even if she enters. [3] His saying: "his stratagem" means the stratagem to avoid the falling of three divorces while she enters the house. [4] His saying: "that he divorce her once" is a conventional restriction; the ruling for two is also similar. That is because if he divorces her once or twice and leaves her without reconciliation until the waiting period expires, then she enters the house while he does not own her, the oath is nullified by the occurrence of the condition, which is entering the house, as mentioned that by the occurrence of the condition the oath dissolves absolutely. Then, if he marries her and she enters the house, nothing occurs due to the nullification of the oath by the occurrence of the condition once, due to the absence of a word indicating continuity. Yes, if he says "Whenever you enter the house, you are divorced," this stratagem does not work. [0] His saying: "and the waiting period ends" indicates that if she enters the house during the waiting period, she becomes irrevocably and severely separated due to the existence of capacity, because ownership remains legally. Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Al-Wiqayah, explaining rulings related to conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) and the effect of dissolution of marriage (zawal al-milk) on such oaths. The commentary begins by addressing a repeated condition phrase: 'Whenever I marry you, you are like this' (i.e., my wife is divorced). The ruling is that each time he marries her, if the condition phrase was said in a way that implies repetition (like 'kullama'), she is divorced each time, even if she was married to another man in between. This is because the oath is ongoing and not dissolved until the condition occurs. The commentary then explains the statement 'Dissolution of marriage does not nullify the oath, and it dissolves after the condition absolutely, and ownership is required for divorce.' The commentator clarifies that the dissolution of the oath (inḥilāl) occurs upon the occurrence of the condition, regardless of whether the condition occurs during marriage (fi al-milk) or after the marriage has ended (ghayr al-milk). However, the effect of the oath—the actual divorce—only takes effect if the condition occurs while the wife is still in the husband's marriage (or during her iddah, as ownership remains legally). If the condition occurs after an irrevocable divorce (ba'in) and the iddah has expired, the oath is dissolved but no divorce occurs because the wife is no longer a valid subject for divorce (lā maḥalliyyah). The hilah (legal stratagem) is presented: a husband who has suspended three divorces on a condition may avoid the triple divorce by divorcing his wife once, allowing her iddah to expire, then she enters the condition (e.g., the house) while not married to him, thereby dissolving the oath. Then he can remarry her, and if she later enters, no divorce occurs. This stratagem works only if the oath is not worded with repetition (e.g., 'kullama'). Finally, the passage addresses a dispute between spouses about the occurrence of the condition: the husband's word (with oath) is taken over the wife's, because he is the denier of the divorce's occurrence, and the principle is that the denier's statement is preferred. This applies to negative conditions as well, such as a condition of non-payment of maintenance.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamīn): The oath or suspension (ta'liq) of divorce or emancipation on a condition. In this context, it refers to the conditional declaration that triggers a consequence upon the occurrence of a future event.
زوال الملك (zawāl al-milk): The dissolution of the marriage bond, specifically by an irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in) or by the expiry of the iddah, removing the husband's ownership over the wife.
انحلال (inḥilāl): The dissolution or nullification of the oath (ta'liq) upon the occurrence of the condition, meaning that the suspension is broken and no longer has effect even if the condition recurs.
المحلية (al-maḥalliyyah): The legal capacity of the wife to be divorced, which requires the existence of a valid marriage (or iddah) at the time the condition occurs.
حيلة (ḥīlah): A legal stratagem or device that uses legitimate means to achieve a permissible result, here to avoid the falling of three divorces while the condition is met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the legal stratagem (hilah) to avoid triple divorce when a husband has suspended three divorces on a condition like 'If you enter the house, you are divorced three times'?
A) Divorce her twice and then let her enter the house immediately.
B) Divorce her once, let her iddah expire, then she enters the house while not married to him, then remarry her.
C) Revoke the conditional statement by saying it was a joke.
D) Marry her to another man and then divorce her.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a conditional divorce oath remains binding until the condition occurs; if the marriage has ended, the oath dissolves without divorce, but a clever stratagem (hilah) can prevent triple divorce.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Disagreement on Condition's Occurrence: Husband's Word Prevails
Establishes the default rule when spouses dispute condition existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on who prevails when spouses dispute the occurrence of a divorce condition, identifying exceptions and the burden of proof.

Core Idea
When spouses dispute a divorce condition, the husband's word is taken because he denies divorce—but with exceptions.

When a husband makes a divorce conditional on a future event, disputes can arise over whether the event occurred. The Hanafi school establishes a clear procedural rule: the husband's statement is preferred with his oath. However, this rule is not absolute. In this lesson, we examine the default principle, the rationale based on the maxim 'al-qawl qawl al-munkir', and the critical exceptions when the wife produces evidence or when only she can know the condition. We also explore an intra-Hanafi refinement regarding maintenance disputes and the requirement of an oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
State the default: when spouses differ on the existence of a condition, the husband's word (with oath) is taken because he is the denier of divorce.
The principle 'al-qawl qawl al-munkir' (the statement of the denier is given weight) is the foundation.
2
Understand the rationale
The husband denies that divorce occurred; the wife claims it did. The burden of proof is on the claimant (wife) and the oath is on the denier (husband).
This is based on the well-known hadith: 'البيّنة على من ادعى واليمين على من أنكر' (The proof is on the claimant and the oath is on the denier).
3
Recognize the exceptions
Two exceptions: (1) If the wife presents evidence (bayyinah), even for a negative condition (e.g., 'if you do not enter'), her word prevails. (2) If the condition is only knowable by her (e.g., subjective states), she is believed in matters concerning her own right.
Evidence is accepted because the real purpose is to prove divorce, not the negative form. For subjective conditions, only she can know.
4
Analyze the maintenance exception
The default rule applies only to non-occurrence of divorce. In disputes over maintenance payment, the wife's word (with oath) is taken because she is the one who knows receipt.
This is a refinement from later Hanafi authorities (al-Bahr, al-Nahr).
5
Examine the oath requirement
When the wife is believed in subjective conditions, there is a dispute: the preferred view (al-Bahr, al-Nahr) says no oath is required; a minority report (Ramz al-Maqdisi) says she must swear.
The mufta bihi is no oath, following the refined view.

[Commentary: [6] His saying: 'And if they differ...' means if the spouses differ over the existence of the condition, with the wife claiming its existence and the husband denying it; what is meant by 'existence' is actual occurrence and establishment, so as to include negative conditions like: 'If you do not enter the house today, you are divorced.' [7] His saying: 'the statement is his' means the husband's statement is taken with his oath, because he is the denier of the occurrence of divorce, and the statement of the denier is given weight. Its apparent meaning implies that if he conditioned her divorce upon her not receiving her maintenance for days, and he claimed delivery and she denied it, the statement would be his; this is explicitly stated in al-Qunyah, quoting al-Muntaga. But this is limited to the matter of non-occurrence of divorce; it is not the case in the matter of receipt of maintenance; rather the wife takes an oath on non-receipt and thus becomes entitled to it. And it is corrected in al-Bahr and al-Nahr and others that in cases of disagreement over the existence of the condition, the husband's statement is taken, except in a matter where he claims fulfillment of a right and she denies, then her statement is taken then.
Except with her proof, and in a condition that cannot be known except from her, she is believed specifically regarding her own right. [Commentary: [1] His saying: 'except with her proof' means unless she presents evidence (bayyinah) for the existence of the condition, even if it is a negative; evidence is accepted regarding the condition, even if it is a negation like 'if my in-law does not come tonight, my wife is divorced'; because although in form it is a negation, in reality it is to prove divorce, and consideration is given to purposes and meanings, not form. So in al-Bahr. [2] His saying: 'and in a condition...' this is an exception to what preceded; the summary is that in cases of disagreement over the existence of the condition, the husband's statement is taken, except in a condition whose existence cannot be known except by her report, then the wife's statement is taken. [3] His saying: 'she is believed' means her words are considered truthful, and divorce is judged to have occurred; this is apparent in judicial and religious matters concerning rulings that depend on her report, as in: 'if you love the punishment of Allah'; but regarding menstruation, she is not divorced religiously unless she is truthful.
And whether the believing here is without an oath or with an oath — what is refined in al-Bahr and al-Nahr is that no oath is upon her; al-Hamawi transmitted from Ramz al-Maqdisi that she must take an oath, as in Radd al-Muhtar.]
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage addresses the procedure when spouses dispute whether the condition of a conditional divorce has occurred. The default rule in the Hanafi school is that the husband's word is taken (with his oath) because he is the denier of the occurrence of divorce, and the principle 'al-qawl qawl al-munkir' (the statement of the denier is given weight) applies. However, there are important exceptions: (1) If the wife produces evidence (bayyinah), her word prevails, even if the condition is a negative one (e.g., 'if you do not enter'), because the evidence is accepted for the reality of proving divorce. (2) If the condition is such that only the wife can know its existence (e.g., subjective states), she is believed specifically regarding her own right. The commentator also clarifies that this rule is limited to divorce; in matters of maintenance (nafaqah), if the husband claims he paid and the wife denies, her word is taken with oath. There is a later discussion about whether the wife needs to take an oath when she is believed; the preferred view (as in al-Bahr and al-Nahr) is that no oath is required, though a minority report she must.

Key Terms
القول قول المنكر (al-qawl qawl al-munkir): A legal maxim that the statement of the party denying a claim is given weight when there is no evidence.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, typically witness testimony, that can prove a fact even if negative in form.
تصديق (taṣdīq): The act of believing a party's statement, here the wife being believed in conditions only she can know.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The wife's, because she is the claimant
B) The husband's, because he is the denier of divorce
C) Both, with equal weight
D) Neither until they produce witnesses

Key Takeaway
The husband's word prevails in conditional divorce disputes unless the wife has evidence or the condition is known only to her.

Credibility in Conditional Divorce: The Exception for Conditions Only the Wife Can Know
Explaining the exception to the husband's credibility when condition is known only by wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the general rule of husband's credibility from the exception for conditions known only by the wife, and state the refined view on oath requirements.

Core Idea
The wife is believed without oath on conditions only she can know — an exception to the husband's credibility in conditional divorce.

In conditional divorce disputes, the default rule is that the husband's word is accepted because he denies divorce and relies on the original state. However, for conditions whose occurrence is inherently private to the wife—such as her love for Allah's punishment—the law makes an exception: her word is believed. This lesson dissects that exception, including the intra-madhhab dispute whether she must take an oath, and clarifies that this belief applies only to her own case (not to associated slaves or other parties).

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
When spouses disagree whether a condition for divorce occurred (e.g., husband denies entering the house, wife claims he did), the husband's word is accepted because he adheres to the original state of no divorce and the apparent circumstance supports him.
This is the default: the one who denies divorce is believed.
2
Recognize the exception
For a 'condition not known except from her' (sharṭ lā yuʿlam illā minhā), such as her love for punishment of Allah, the wife's word is accepted over the husband's. This is an exception because she has exclusive knowledge.
Example: 'If you love the punishment of Allah, you are divorced.'
3
Understand the scope: qada'an and diyanatan
The belief applies both judicially (qada'an) and religiously (diyanatan) for conditions like love of punishment. However, for menstruation, she is not believed religiously unless she is truthful; judicially she is believed.
The nuance is that diyanatan she must be truthful for her own conscience, but the court accepts her word.
4
Determine the oath requirement
The refined view (from al-Bahr and al-Nahr) is that no oath is required of her. But Ramz al-Maqdisi transmitted that she must take an oath. The mufta bihi is no oath.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; the later authorities prefer no oath.
5
Note the limitation: only in her right
The belief applies 'fi haqqihā khāṣṣatan' – only regarding her own case. If the same condition was attached to freeing a slave, her word does not free the slave; only she is divorced.
The passage ends: 'if she says: I menstruated and I love it, only she is divorced'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي شرط لا يعلمُ إلا منها صُدِّقَت في حقها خاصة. (1) أي إن اختلف الزوجان في وجود الشرط بأن قال الزوج: ما دخلت الدار، وقالت المرأة: بل دخلتها، فالقول للزوج؛ لأنه متمسك بالأصل فكان الظاهر شاهد له، ولأنه ينكر وقوع الطلاق وهي تدعيه إلا إذا أقامت البينة على دعواها. ثم قال: وفي شرط لا يعلم إلا منها صدقت في حقها خاصة. هذا كالاستثناء مما سبق، وحاصله: أن في صور اختلاف الزوجين في وجود الشرط يكون القول قوله، إلا في شرط لا يعلم وجوده إلا بإخبارها، فإن القول حينئذ قول المرأة. قال: صدقت؛ أي اعتبر كلامها صادقاً، وحكم بوقوع الطلاق، وهذا ظاهر قضاء وديانةً فيما يترتب الحكم فيه على إخبارها كما في: إن كنت تحبين عذاب الله، وأما في الحيض فلا تطلق ديانة إلا إذا كانت صادقة. وهل يكون التصديق هاهنا بدون اليمين أو مع اليمين؟ الذي نقحه في «البحر» و«النهر» هو أن لا يمين عليها. نقل الحموي عن «رمز المقدسي»: إن عليها اليمين. كذا في «رد المحتار». وفي: إن كنت تحبين عذاب الله، فأنت كذا، وعبده حر، لو قالت: حضت وأحبه طلقت هي فقط.
And regarding a condition that is not known except from her, she is believed specifically regarding her own case. (1) That is, if the spouses disagree about the occurrence of the condition—the husband saying 'I did not enter the house' and the wife saying 'You entered it'—the word is that of the husband because he adheres to the original state, so the apparent circumstance supports him, and because he denies the occurrence of divorce while she claims it, unless she produces evidence for her claim. Then he said: 'And regarding a condition that is not known except from her, she is believed specifically regarding her own case.' This is like an exception to what preceded. Its summary: In cases of disagreement between spouses about the occurrence of the condition, the husband's word is accepted, except for a condition whose existence is not known except through her report; then the wife's word is accepted. He said: 'She is believed'—i.e., her statement is considered truthful, and divorce is ruled to have occurred. This is apparent in judicial matters and religiously regarding what is based on her report, as in 'If you love the punishment of Allah.' As for menstruation, she is not divorced religiously unless she is truthful. And is this acceptance without an oath or with an oath? What is refined in al-Bahr and al-Nahr is that she is not required to take an oath. Al-Hamawi transmitted from Ramz al-Maqdisi that she is required to take an oath, as in Radd al-Muhtar. And in: 'If you love the punishment of Allah, then you are such and such, and my slave is free,' if she says 'I have menstruated and I love it,' only she is divorced.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation regarding credibility in disputes over the occurrence of a condition in conditional divorce. The author first reiterates the general rule: when spouses disagree whether a condition occurred (e.g., husband says 'I did not enter the house' and wife says 'you did'), the husband's word is accepted because he adheres to the original state of no divorce, and the burden of proof is on the one claiming the divorce. Then an exception is made: for conditions whose existence is known only by the wife, such as her love for Allah's punishment, her word is accepted. The commentary clarifies that this is in judicial matters and also in religious practice (diyanatan) regarding what depends on her report, but for menstruation, religiously she is not believed unless she is truthful. There is also a dispute whether she must take an oath; the author prefers the view that no oath is required, but cites a contrary opinion.

صُدِّقَت (suddiaqat): She is believed; her statement is accepted as truthful in legal ruling.
شرط لا يعلم إلا منها (sharṭ lā yuʿlam illā minhā): A condition whose occurrence is known only by the wife, e.g., her love for Allah's punishment.

Retroactive Divorce upon Condition of Menstruation
Explains when divorce takes effect if conditioned on menstruation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a conditional divorce based on menstruation takes effect and apply the rule to scenarios involving timing.

Core Idea
Divorce conditioned on menstruation takes effect retroactively only after three days of blood.

When a husband says 'If you menstruate, you are divorced,' the divorce does not happen upon first spotting. The Hanafi school requires three days of continuous blood to confirm true menstruation (hayd), and then the divorce is applied back to the first day. This retroactive ruling affects 'iddah, remarriage, and even manumission of slaves. Let's unpack the reasoning and application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its ruling
The husband's statement 'If you menstruate, you are divorced' is a conditional divorce (ta'liq). The act of menstruation (hayd) is the condition. The ruling is that after three days of continuous blood, the divorce is effective retroactively from the first day.
The condition must be clearly expressed and the blood must be genuine hayd, not istihadah.
2
Understand the need for a three-day confirmation
Hanafi fiqh defines hayd as blood that lasts at least three days. Therefore, upon seeing less than three days, it is not hayd and the condition is not fulfilled. The three-day period proves the blood is menstrual.
If blood stops before three days, it is istihadah (non-menstrual) and no divorce occurs.
3
Apply retroactivity
Once three days pass, the divorce is deemed to have occurred from the first day of blood, not from the moment after three days. This means the legal effects (like divorce, start of 'iddah, or freeing a slave) are backdated.
This may affect remarriage: if the woman remarried during those three days, the second marriage might be invalid because she was already divorced from the first day.
4
Examine the linguistic clarification
The text clarifies that 'ḥayḍah' (with fatha) means one occurrence of menstruation. This is the word used in the condition. The plural 'ḥiyāḍ' is for multiple instances. The distinction matters for precise wording in divorce conditions.
Knowing the exact term helps avoid ambiguity in contracts.
5
Analyze the practical applications given
Two applications: (1) An unconsummated woman sees blood, then marries another. After three days, the divorce is retroactive, so she was divorced from first day; since unconsummated, no 'iddah, so second marriage is valid. (2) If the condition was freeing a slave, the slave is free from first day, so any earnings during those days belong to the slave.
These examples show the real impact of retroactivity on rights and obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففي: إن حضتِ فأنت طالق. قال: وفي: إن حضت يُحكم بالجزاء بعد رؤية الدم ثلاثة أيام من أوله. أي إن قال: إن حضت فأنت كذا، فبعدما رأت الدم ثلاثة أيام يُحكم بالجزاء من أول الدم؛ لأنه تبين برؤية الدم ثلاثة أيام أنه حيض، فيحكم بعد الثلاثة بوقوع الجزاء في أولها. وفي: إن حضت حيضة. قوله: حيضة؛ هو بالفتح اسم للمرة الواحدة من الحيض، وأما الحيضة بالكسر، فهو اسم، وجمعه حيض. كذا في «صحاح الجوهري». قوله: من أوله؛ أي من حين ابتداء جريان دمها لا من حين انقطاعه، ولا فيما بين ذلك. قال في «النهاية»: فائدة هذا تظهر فيما إذا كانت المرأة غير مدخول بها، فإنها لما رأت دماً وتزوجت بزوج آخر واستمر بها الدم ثلاثة أيام، كان النكاح صحيحاً لانقطاعها من الزوج بأول ما رأت لا إلى عدة. وتظهر أيضاً فيما إذا قال: إن حضت فعبدي حر، والمسألة بحالها كان العبد حراً من حين رأت الدم، حتى كان الاكتساب للعبد. انتهى.
So in: 'If you menstruate, you are divorced.' He said: And in: 'If you menstruate, the consequence is judged after seeing blood for three days from its beginning.' That is, if he said: 'If you menstruate, you are such and such,' then after she sees blood for three days, the consequence is judged from the first of the blood, because it becomes clear by seeing blood for three days that it is menstruation; so after three days, it is ruled that the consequence occurred at its beginning. And in: 'If you menstruate one menstruation.' He said: 'حيضة' (hayda) is with fatha, a noun for a single instance of menstruation; as for 'حيضة' (hayda) with kasra, it is a noun, and its plural is 'حيض' (hiyad). This is from Sahih al-Jawhari. He said: 'from its beginning' means from the time the blood starts flowing, not from the time it stops, nor in between. He said in al-Nihaya: The benefit of this appears in the case of a woman who is not yet consummated; if she sees blood and marries another husband and the blood continues for three days, the marriage is valid because she separated from the first husband from the first sighting of blood, not waiting for a waiting period. It also appears if he said: 'If you menstruate, my slave is free,' and the case is the same, the slave becomes free from the time she sees blood, so that any earnings belong to the slave. End.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation regarding the timing of divorce when conditioned on menstruation. The author explains the matn: if a husband says 'If you menstruate, you are divorced,' the divorce does not take effect immediately upon seeing blood; rather, after three days of continuous blood (proving it is true hayd), the divorce is applied retroactively from the first day of blood. The reasoning is that only after three days is it established that the blood was menstruation, not istihadah (non-menstrual bleeding). Then the author discusses the phrase 'one menstruation' (hayda with fatha) and clarifies the linguistic difference. The benefit of retroactivity is shown in two cases: (1) if the woman is unconsummated and remarries before the three days, the divorce is effective from the first blood, so her waiting period ('iddah) begins then, and remarriage is invalid if she already has a waiting period? Actually, the text says the marriage is valid because she separated from the first husband from the first blood not needing a waiting period? Wait, the translation says: 'the marriage is valid because she separated from the first husband from the first sighting of blood, not waiting for a waiting period.' This needs careful reading: if the woman is unconsummated and sees blood, and then marries another before three days prove it is hayd, once three days pass, it is known she was menstruating and thus divorced from the first husband from the first day, so the second marriage is invalid because she was still in the 'iddah of the first? Actually, it says 'the marriage is valid' (صحيحاً) because she was separated from the first husband immediately without an 'iddah? That seems contradictory. Let me re-read the Arabic: 'كان النكاح صحيحاً لانقطاعها من الزوج بأول ما رأت لا إلى عدة.' This means: the marriage (with the second husband) is valid because she separated from the first husband from the first blood she saw, without needing to wait for an 'iddah (since she was unconsummated, no 'iddah). So the divorce from the first husband is effective from the first blood, so she is free to remarry immediately. The second marriage is valid. The commentary also gives the case of a slave: if the condition was that a slave be freed upon her menstruation, the slave becomes free from the first blood, so any earnings during those three days belong to the slave.

Key Terms
حيضة (ḥayḍah): A single instance of menstruation; here with fatha on ḥā.
من أوله (min awwalihi): From the beginning of the blood flow, not from its end or middle.
حيض (hayḍ): Menstruation as a phenomenon; also the plural of ḥayḍah.

Key Takeaway
A divorce conditioned on menstruation takes effect retroactively from the first day of blood only after three continuous days prove it is hayd.

Conditional Divorce with Multiple Conditions and Acceptance of Implausible Claims
Ruling on conditional divorce with multiple conditions and acceptance of her claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce (talaq mu'allaq) when multiple conditions are combined (laff) and explain why a wife's claim of loving punishment is accepted despite apparent implausibility.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a wife's claim of loving Allah's punishment is accepted if motivated by hatred of her husband.

This passage from al-Wiqayah and its commentary presents a nuanced scenario of conditional divorce. A husband suspends multiple effects on his wife's menstruation and her love of punishment. The wife claims both conditions are met, including the implausible claim of loving punishment. The ruling clarifies that only her own divorce takes effect, and her claim about loving punishment is accepted due to her intense hatred for her husband, which may make punishment desirable as a means of escape.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions and their objects
The husband's conditional statement includes: (1) if you (the wife) menstruate, you are divorced; (2) if a named other woman menstruates, she is divorced; (3) if you love Allah's punishment, a penalty applies; (4) his slave is freed. Each condition is linked to a specific effect.
2
Determine the wife's claim
The wife states: 'I menstruated and I love (Allah's punishment).' She claims both the condition for her own divorce and the condition for the penalty.
3
Analyze the objection
A potential objection arises: normally, a claim that a person loves punishment is inherently unbelievable because no rational person loves punishment. So how can the wife's statement be accepted?
The objection assumes general human nature, but the context of marital discord creates an exception.
4
Apply the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that due to the wife's severe hatred of her husband, she may genuinely love punishment as a means of deliverance (takhallus) from the marriage. Hence her claim is accepted in this specific context, as recorded in al-Nihayah.
This ruling is an exception (istithna') based on the wife's likely motivation, not a general principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ففي : إن حضتِ فأنت طالق ، وفلانة، وإن كنت تحبين عذاب الله ، فأنت كذا، وعبده حرّ، لو قالت : حضت وأحبه طلقت هي فقط. [1]قوله : وأحبه ؛ أي عذاب الله ، قد يقال : إنما يقبلُ قولها في شرط لا يعلمُ إلا من جهتها باعتبار الصدق وفي الإخبار المحبّة عذاب الله الكذب معلوم بظهور أنّ إنسانا لا يحب عذابه، فكيف يقبل قولها هاهنا ؟ ويجاب عنه : بأنها لشدة بغضها زوجها قد تحب التخلّص . منه بالعذاب. كذا في «النهاية».
So in it: If you menstruate then you are divorced, and so-and-so, and if you love the punishment of Allah then such-and-such, and his slave is free. If she said: I menstruated and I love it, she is divorced only. [1] His saying: And I love it; i.e. the punishment of Allah. It may be said: Her statement is accepted only in a condition that is not known except from her side with consideration of truthfulness, but in reporting love of Allah's punishment, lying is known by the appearance that no human loves His punishment, so how is her statement accepted here? And it is answered: Because of her intense hatred of her husband, she may love deliverance through punishment. Thus in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn of al-Wiqayah along with the sharh commentary. The husband makes a conditional divorce with multiple conditions linked to his wife: (1) if she menstruates, she is divorced; (2) if a named other woman menstruates, she is divorced; (3) if she loves the punishment of Allah, she is subject to a penalty; (4) his slave is freed. The wife then claims both that she menstruated and that she loves Allah's punishment. The ruling is that only she herself is divorced, not the other woman or the slave. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: normally one would not believe a woman's claim that she loves Allah's punishment, since no sane person loves punishment. The answer is that because of her intense hatred for her husband, she may actually love the punishment as a means of escape from the marriage. Therefore her claim is accepted in this context, as stated in al-Nihayah.

Key Terms
اللف (al-laff): The combination of multiple conditions or consequences in a single conditional phrase.
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Exception; here, it refers to the nuanced exception in the ruling where claims are accepted despite apparent implausibility.
