Conditional Divorce Tied to Menstruation: The Three-Day Rule
Ruling that conditional divorce tied to menstruation takes effect after three days of blood, retroactively.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a conditional divorce tied to menstruation takes effect and apply it to scenarios involving unconsummated marriage and conditional emancipation.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce tied to menstruation only takes effect after three days of blood, retroactively from day one.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding conditional divorces where the condition is 'if you menstruate.' The ruling clarifies that the conditional effect—whether divorce or emancipation—is not immediate upon seeing blood. Instead, it is suspended until the blood has flowed for three continuous days, which confirms it as true menstruation (hayd). Thereafter, the effect is applied retroactively from the first day of bleeding. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly determining marital status, iddah, and ownership of earnings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its suspension
The condition is 'if you menstruate.' The effect is suspended until it is certain that menstruation occurred. Explain that the effect is not immediate.
2
Define the minimum duration of hayd
In the Hanafi school, the minimum duration of menstruation is three days. Bleeding less than three days is istihadah (irregular bleeding) and does not count.
Refer to the chapter of hayd.
3
Apply the three-day confirmation
After three full days of continuous blood, the condition is deemed fulfilled. The effect (divorce, emancipation) is then judged to have occurred from the beginning of the blood flow.
4
Examine the retroactivity
The effect is retroactive to the first day, not from the third day. This has practical consequences for iddah and ownership.
The text says 'من أوله' (from its beginning).
5
Apply to two scenarios
Work through the two examples from al-Nihayah: (a) unconsummated woman who remarries during the three days; (b) slave emancipation conditional on menstruation.
In each case, the effect retroacts to the first day of blood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي : إن حضت يُحكم بالجزاء بعد رؤية الدم ثلاثة أيام من أوله ) : أي إن قال : إن حضت فأنت كذا، فبعدما رأتِ الدَّمَ ثلاثةَ أَيَّام يُحكم بالجزاء من أوَّلِ الدَّم ؛ لأنه تَبَيَّنَ برؤية الدم ثلاثة أيام أنه حيض، فيُحكم بعد الثلاثةِ بوقوع الجزاء في أولها. [2] قوله : بعد رؤية الدم ثلاثة أيام لا قبلها يجوز أن ينقطع الدم فيما دون الثلاث، وما ينقطع دون الثلاث ليس بحيض بل استحاضة كما مر في باب الحيض. [3]قوله : من أوّله ؛ أي من حين ابتداء جريان دمها لا من حين انقطاعه، ولا فيما بين ذلك، قال في «النهاية»: فائدة هذا تظهرُ فيما إذا كانت المرأة غير مدخول بها، فإنّها لَمَّا رأت دماً وتزوجت بزوج آخر واستمرَّ بها الدم ثلاثة أيام، كان النكاح صحيحاً لانقطاعها من الزوج بأول ما رأت لا إلى عدة. وتظهر أيضاً فيما إذا قال: إن حضت فعبدي حرّ، والمسألة بحالها كان العبد حراً من حين رأت الدم، حتى كان الاكتساب للعبد. انتهى.
And in it: If you menstruate, judgment is made with the consequence after seeing blood for three days from its beginning. That is, if he said: If you menstruate then you are such-and-such, then after she sees blood for three days, judgment is made with the consequence from the beginning of the blood; because it becomes evident by seeing blood for three days that it is menstruation, so judgment is made after the three that the consequence occurred at its beginning. [2] His saying: after seeing blood for three days, not before, because it is possible that the blood stops before three, and what stops before three is not menstruation but istihadah, as passed in the chapter of hayd. [3] His saying: from its beginning; i.e., from the time of the start of the flow of her blood, not from its cessation, nor between that. He said in al-Nihayah: The benefit of this appears in the case when the woman is not yet consummated, for when she saw blood and married another husband and the blood continued for three days, the marriage is valid because she is separated from the first husband from the first sight of blood without waiting for an iddah. And it also appears in the case when he said: If you menstruate, my slave is free, and the issue is similar: the slave is free from the time she saw blood, so that the earnings are for the slave. End.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage deals with conditional divorce where the condition is 'if you menstruate'. Since menstruation is not confirmed until blood flows for at least three continuous days, the conditional effect (e.g., divorce) is only judged after three days have passed, but it applies retroactively from the first day of blood. The commentator explains why we cannot judge earlier: because blood may stop before three days, which would be istihadah (irregular bleeding) and not true menstruation. Two practical applications are given: (1) for an unconsummated woman, if she sees blood and marries another, the marriage is valid because she is considered separated from the first husband from the first sight, without needing an iddah period; (2) for emancipation conditional on menstruation, the slave is free from the first day of blood, so any earnings during those three days belong to the slave.

Key Terms
الجزاء (al-jaza'): The consequence or result of the conditional statement, here divorce or emancipation.
الاستحاضة (al-istihadah): Irregular non-menstrual bleeding that does not count as hayd.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Conditional Divorce with 'A Menstruation' Requires Completion
Ruling on timing of conditional divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a conditional divorce phrased with 'haydah' takes effect and explain why purification is required.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce using 'a menstruation' requires the full cycle to end with purification before it occurs.

In Hanafi law, the wording of a conditional divorce matters immensely. When a husband says 'if you menstruate a menstruation,' the term 'haydah' indicates a complete menstrual period—from start to purification. This lesson unpacks that linguistic nuance and its legal consequence: the divorce is suspended until the wife becomes pure, ensuring it falls in a state of tuhr, making it a proper sunni divorce rather than a bid'i one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Conditional Phrase
Locate the husband's statement: 'If you menstruate a menstruation (haydah), you are divorced.' The key word is 'haydah' (with fatha), which the commentator distinguishes from the generic 'hayd'.
The distinction is based on Arabic morphology: haydah = one instance; hayd = the phenomenon.
2
Define 'Haydah'
Understand that 'haydah' means one complete menstrual cycle, not just the onset of bleeding. It ends with purification (tuhr), either by natural cessation (after up to ten days) or by ghusl and passing of a prayer time.
This definition is supported by the hadith on istibra' cited in the commentary.
3
Determine When Purification Occurs
Purification (tuhr) is confirmed either by the end of ten days if bleeding continues, or by performing ghusl and allowing time for one prayer if bleeding stops earlier. The divorce takes effect at that moment, not before.
This ensures the divorce occurs in a state of purity, avoiding bid'ah.
4
Apply the Timing of Divorce
The divorce is suspended until purification is complete. If the wife begins menstruating, the divorce does not occur until she becomes pure. This is the direct ruling of the passage.
If the husband had said 'if you menstruate' (using 'hayd' or 'tahidina'), the ruling might differ.
5
Distinguish Sunni vs. Bid'i Divorce
A divorce that takes effect during purity is sunni (proper); one during menstruation is bid'i (innovative). The condition of purification ensures the divorce is sunni.
The Hanafi school strongly encourages sunni divorce to avoid harm to the woman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي : إن حضت حيضة لا يقع حتى تطهر. [4] قوله حيضة ; هو بالفتح اسم للمرّة الواحدة من الحيض، وأما الحيضة بالكسر ، فهو اسم ، وجمعه حيض. كذا في «صحاح الجوهري». لا يقع حتى تطهر ; فإنَّ الحيضة هي الكاملة. [2] قوله : حتى تطهر، إما بالانقطاع بمضي عشرة أيام، وإما بالاغتسال ومضي وقت صلاة فيما دونها، فحينئذ يقع الطلاق، ويكون سنيّاً لا بدعياً؛ لوقوعه في الطهر. [3] قوله : هي بكماله، وكماله يكون بانتهائه، وهو بالطهر، وعليه حملت الحيضة الواردة في حديث : «لا توطأ الحبالى حتى يضعن حملهن، ولا غير الحبالى حتى تستبرأن بحيضة».
And in it: If you menstruate a menstruation, it does not occur until she becomes pure. [4] His saying: haydah; it is with fatha, a noun for a single instance of menstruation. As for haydah with kasra, it is a noun, and its plural is hayd. Thus in Sahih al-Jawhari. It does not occur until she becomes pure; because 'a menstruation' is the complete one. [2] His saying: until she becomes pure, either by cessation after ten days, or by ghusl and the passing of a prayer time if less than ten. Then divorce occurs and is sunni, not bid'i, because it occurs in a state of purity. [3] His saying: it is with its completion, and its completion is by its end, which is purification. And upon this the 'haydah' in the hadith is interpreted: 'Pregnant women are not to be taken in marriage until they deliver, and non-pregnant women not until they are cleared by one menstruation.'
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage explains the difference between the general term 'hayd' (menstruation) and the specific 'haydah' (one menstruation). When the husband says 'if you menstruate a menstruation' (حيضة), the divorce does not take effect until she becomes pure (i.e., completes the menstrual period and performs ghusl or enough time passes for a prayer). This is because 'a menstruation' means the full cycle from start to purification. The commentator notes that purification can occur either by the natural cessation of blood after a maximum of ten days, or by ghusl and the passing of a prayer time if bleeding stops earlier. This ensures the divorce occurs in a state of purity (tuhr), making it a sunni (proper) divorce rather than a bid'i (innovative) one. The hadith about istibra' (waiting for one menstruation) is cited as evidence that 'haydah' refers to the complete cycle.

Key Terms
الحيضة (al-haydah): A single complete menstrual period from start to purification.
الطهر (al-tuhr): The state of purity after menstruation, confirmed by cessation of blood and ghusl or passage of a prayer time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Immediately upon seeing blood.
B) After three days of blood.
C) Only after she completes the menstruation and becomes pure.
D) After the blood stops, even without ghusl.

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce using 'haydah' requires the wife to complete her menstruation and become pure before the divorce takes effect, ensuring it is a sunni divorce.

Conditional Divorce: The Effect of Specifying a Measure
Ruling on conditional divorce with fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conditional divorce statements that specify a measured unit and those that do not, and determine when divorce occurs based on Hanafi linguistic principles.

Core Idea
Specified measure requires completion; unspecified triggers with minimum valid act.

The wording of a conditional divorce is critical. A husband may say 'if you fast a day' or simply 'if you fast'. The Hanafi school applies a linguistic principle: when a measured unit (like 'day' or 'prayer') is mentioned, the condition is fulfilled only upon completion of that unit. Without a measure, the minimum legally valid act suffices. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its underlying analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional phrases
The text contrasts 'if you fast a day' (صمت يوماً) with 'if you fast' (إن صمت) and also mentions 'if you fast a fast' (إن صمت صوماً). Note the presence or absence of a specific measure.
A 'day' is a complete unit from dawn to sunset.
2
Determine the trigger for each phrase
For 'if you fast a day', divorce occurs at sunset of the day she fasts (full completion). For 'if you fast', divorce occurs as soon as she performs a legally valid fast, even for an hour. For 'if you fast a fast', divorce occurs only after completing a full day because 'a fast' is measured by a standard.
The measure is explicit in the phrase.
3
Understand the legal basis
The ruling rests on the principle that when a condition includes a measured unit, it is a complete requirement. Without a unit, the minimum valid act is sufficient. This is supported by qiyas (analogy) to prayer: 'if you pray a prayer' requires two rak'ahs, while 'if you pray' occurs with one rak'ah.
The analogy highlights consistency across ibadat.
4
Apply the principle to similar phrases
If the husband says 'if you fast in a day' or 'in a month', the divorce occurs upon any valid fast within that period, because 'in' does not specify completion. The key is whether the condition expects a full unit.
Prepositions like 'in' change the scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي : إن صُمْتِ يوماً فأنت طالق، تطلق حين غربت من يوم صامت، بخلاف : إن صمت، فإنه يقع على صوم ساعة. [3] قوله : حين غربت ; أي يقعُ الطلاقُ عليها حين غروب الشمس يوم صومها، ونظيره : إن صمت، صوماً فلا يقع إلا بتمام يوم ; لأنه مقدر بمعيار بخلاف : إن صمت، فإنه يتعلّق الطلاق حينئذ بما يسمّى صوماً شرعاً، وقد وجد بركنه، وشرطه بإمساك ساعة فيقع به، وإن قطعه بعدها، وكذا : إن صمت في يوم أو في شهر ; لأنه لم يشترط كماله، وفي : إذا صليت صلاة فأنت طالق، يقع الطلاق بركعتين، وفي : إذا صليت يقعُ بركعة. كذا في «الفتح».
And in it: If you fast a day then you are divorced, she is divorced at sunset of the day she fasted, contrary to: If you fast, then it occurs upon fasting for an hour. [3] His saying: at sunset; i.e., the divorce occurs upon her at the sunset of the day she fasts. Its analogy: if he says 'if you fast a fast' it does not occur except with completion of the day; because it is measured by a standard. Contrary to: if he says 'if you fast', then the divorce attaches to what is called fasting legally, and it is found by its pillar and condition by refraining for an hour, so it occurs by it even if she breaks after that. Similarly, if he says 'if you fast in a day' or 'in a month', because he did not stipulate its completion. And if he says 'if you pray a prayer then you are divorced', divorce occurs with two rak'ahs; and if he says 'if you pray', it occurs with one rak'ah. Thus in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage distinguishes two types of conditional divorce related to fasting. If the husband says 'if you fast a day', the divorce occurs only at the sunset of that day, because a 'day' is a complete unit from dawn to sunset. If he simply says 'if you fast', then the divorce occurs as soon as she performs any legally valid fast, even if only for an hour, because the word 'fast' without qualification applies to the minimum act. The commentator analogizes to prayer: 'if you pray a prayer' requires at least two rak'ahs (a complete prayer), while 'if you pray' occurs with one rak'ah. This is from al-Fath. The key principle is that when a specific measure is mentioned, it must be complete; otherwise, the minimum valid act suffices.

Key Terms
الصوم (al-sawm): Fasting; legally, abstinence from food, drink, and marital relations with intention.
اليوم (al-yawm): A full day from dawn to sunset; a complete unit.
صوم ساعة (sawm sa'ah): Fasting for an hour; a minimal valid fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says, 'If you fast, you are divorced.' The wife fasts for one hour then breaks. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) No divorce, because she did not complete the day.
B) Divorce occurs because she performed a legally valid fast.
C) Divorce occurs only if she fasts the whole day.
D) Divorce occurs at sunset regardless.

Key Takeaway
When a conditional divorce includes a measured unit (e.g., 'a day'), completion is required; otherwise, the minimum valid act triggers.

Conditional Divorce on Gender with Twins: Qada vs. Tanzuh
Ruling on conditional divorce with twins, qada and tanzuh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the judicial ruling (qada) and pious recommendation (tanzuh) in a case of conditional divorce with twins and apply the ruling to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
When uncertainty exists, the court rules on certainty, but the husband must act piously on possibility.

This lesson explores a nuanced Hanafi ruling where a husband suspends divorce on the child's gender but the wife gives birth to twins of both genders in unknown order. The court (qada) can only confirm one divorce due to certainty, but the husband's religious duty (tanzuh) requires him to observe two divorces. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying Hanafi rules of conditional talaq and the role of certainty in legal judgments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its gender-based variation
The husband said: one talaq upon birth of a male, two talaq upon birth of a female. This creates two possible conditions.
2
Recognize the factual uncertainty
She gave birth to twins, one male and one female, and it is unknown which was born first. This triggers ambiguity about which condition occurred.
3
Determine the judicial approach (qada)
The court rules based on certainty. At least one divorce is certain (because either condition triggers at least one talaq). The court cannot enforce two because it is not certain that the female was born first. Thus, judicially, one talaq.
Qada relies on apparent evidence; doubt prevents enforcement of a higher number.
4
Determine the religious approach (tanzuh/diyanah)
Between the husband and Allah, he must consider that the female may have been born first, making two divorces due. To avoid the sin of remaining married after a possible third talaq (if two have occurred), he should treat it as two divorces piously.
Tanzuh is a recommendation of caution in doubtful matters.
5
Note the practical consequence
The marriage is dissolved by one revocable divorce in court, but the husband must consider himself divorced twice in his conscience. This affects future marital possibilities (e.g., if he remarries, he must treat it as after irrevocable divorce).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو علق طلقة بولادة ذكر وطلقتين بأنثى فولدتهما، ولم يُدْرَ الأوّلُ طُلقت واحدةً قضاء وثنتين تَنَزُّهاً ) : أي ديانة : يعني فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى .
And if he suspended a single divorce upon the birth of a male and two divorces upon the birth of a female, and she gave birth to both, and it is not known which was first: she is divorced one in qada and two in tanzuh. That is, diyanatan, meaning between him and Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage addresses a case where the husband suspends different numbers of divorces on the gender of the child: one talaq if she gives birth to a male, two if to a female. The wife gives birth to twins (one male, one female) and it is unknown which was born first. The ruling distinguishes between the judicial domain (qada, i.e., court ruling) and the religious domain (tanzuh/diyanah, i.e., what is required between the husband and Allah). Qadaan, the court rules that one divorce has occurred, because it is certain that at least one child was born first, and whichever it was, the condition for one divorce is fulfilled. For tanzuh (piety), the husband must consider that two divorces may have occurred (if the female was born first), so he should treat it as two divorces in his private conscience, though the court cannot enforce that due to uncertainty.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Judicial ruling based on apparent evidence; here, the court rules one divorce due to certainty.
تنزهاً (tanzuhan): Piously, as a matter of religious caution; here, the husband should consider himself divorced twice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says 'if you give birth to a son, you are divorced once; if to a daughter, divorced twice.' She gives birth to twins (boy and girl) and the order is unknown. What is the judicial ruling?
A) One divorce.
B) Two divorces.
C) Three divorces.
D) No divorce.

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce with twins of unknown order, the Hanafi school rules one divorce judicially but recommends two piously.

Divorce Conditional on Menstruation Until Purification
Ruling with proof on conditional divorce timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for divorce to take effect in a case of conditional divorce during menstruation and apply the Hanafi definition of a complete menstruation (haydah) to determine the moment of validity.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce uttered during menstruation only takes effect upon complete purity – ghusl or end of ten days.

This passage clarifies a key ruling in Hanafi divorce law: when a husband conditions divorce on his wife's menstruation, the divorce does not occur immediately upon the start of bleeding nor upon its mere cessation. Instead, the school teaches that the divorce becomes effective only when she attains full purity (taharah). This ruling is linked to the broader principle of istibra' (ensuring the womb is empty) and is derived from a hadith. Let us unpack the conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a conditional divorce uttered while the wife is menstruating. The ruling is that it does not take effect until she becomes pure (taharah).
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites the hadith: 'لا توطأ الحبالى حتى يضعن حملهن، ولا غير الحبالى حتى تستبرأن بحيضة' (pregnant women are not to be approached until they deliver, and non-pregnant women until they are cleared by one menstruation). This hadith is used to define the complete menstruation (haydah) as ending with purity.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'حيضة' (haydah) in the hadith is interpreted as a complete menstruation cycle. The commentator argues that the completion (كمال) of haydah is its end, which is purity (طهر). Thus, the condition in the conditional divorce is fulfilled only when purity occurs.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools may differ on the precise moment of purity. The Hanafi position is explicit: purity is achieved either by the completion of ten days (maximum duration) without ghusl, or by performing ghusl and the passing of a prayer time if the bleeding stopped earlier.
5
Evaluate the practice
The divorce is effective upon purity, and it is considered sunni (proper) because it occurs in a state of purity. The classification as sunni has implications for revocability and subsequent actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقع حتى تطهر)؛ فإنَّ الحيضة هي الكاملة. [1] قوله : حتى تطهر، إما بالانقطاع بمضي عشرة أيام، وإما بالاغتسال ومضي وقت صلاة فيما دونها ، فحينئذ يقع الطلاق، ويكون سنيّاً لا بدعياً؛ لوقوعه في الطهر. [2] قوله : هي بكماله، وكماله يكون بانتهائه، وهو بالطهر، وعليه حملت الحيضة الواردة في حديث : لا توطأ الحبالى حتى يضعن حملهن، ولا غير الحبالى حتى تستبرأن بحيضة»، أخرجه أبو داود، وغيره.
It does not occur until she becomes pure; for the menstruation (haydah) is the complete one. [1] His saying: 'until she becomes pure' – either by cessation after ten days, or by ghusl and the passing of a prayer time in less than that. Then at that point divorce occurs, and it is sunni, not bid'i, because it occurs in a state of purity. [2] His saying: 'it (haydah) is its completion' – its completion is at its end, which is purity. Upon this is based the haydah mentioned in the hadith: 'Pregnant women are not to be approached until they deliver, and non-pregnant women until they are cleared by one menstruation (haydah),' narrated by Abu Dawud and others.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the hadith. The main ruling states that when a husband says to his menstruating wife 'you are divorced,' the divorce does not take effect until she becomes pure. The commentator explains that purity is achieved either by the end of ten days of menstruation (the maximum) or by ghusl after bleeding stops and the passing of a prayer time for less than ten days. At that point, the divorce falls and is considered sunni (proper) because it occurs in a state of purity, not bid'i (innovative). The term 'haydah' in the hadith refers to a complete menstruation – it is understood that istibra' (clearing the womb) requires a full menstrual cycle, which ends with purity. This is consistent with the Hanafi position that a single menstruation is a full cycle.

Key Terms
حيضة (haydah): A complete menstruation cycle, beginning with bleeding and ending with purity (taharah).
سني (sunni): A divorce that occurs in a state of purity without having intercourse during that purity, considered proper.
بدعي (bid'i): An improper divorce that occurs during menstruation or in a state of impurity after intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately when bleeding stops
B) When she becomes completely pure by ghusl or the end of her maximum term
C) After she has one more menstruation
D) Only if she enters a new menstrual cycle

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce uttered during menstruation is effective only upon complete purity (taharah), ensuring it is sunni (proper) and based on a complete menstruation cycle.

Divorce Conditional on Fasting: 'A Day' vs 'Fasting'
Distinguishing between general and specific conditional terms in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the difference in timing of conditional divorce when the phrase includes 'a day' versus an unqualified 'fasting', and apply the ruling to analogous conditional expressions.

Core Idea
If the condition specifies a day, divorce requires its completion; if unqualified, any legal act of fasting triggers it.

This passage from the commentary on the Wiqayah clarifies how a husband's conditional wording affects the timing of divorce. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying similar conditional expressions in marriage contracts and beyond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional phrases
The passage contrasts 'if you fast a day' and 'if you fast'. Note the difference: one includes 'a day', the other lacks any time qualifier.
The Arabic adds a definite article to 'fast' in the second phrase.
2
Determine the timing of divorce for each
For 'a day', divorce occurs at sunset of that day. For unqualified 'fasting', it occurs upon any act legally recognized as fasting, even for an hour.
The text uses 'hour' as a minimal duration.
3
Understand the reason for the difference
The day is a complete unit (mi'yar), so the condition requires fullness. The unqualified term only requires the legal definition of fasting.
Hanafi principle: definite terms indicate a bounded condition; indefinite terms indicate minimal fulfillment.
4
Extend the reasoning to analogous expressions
The passage gives examples with prayer: 'if you pray a prayer' requires two rak'ahs; 'if you pray' requires only one rak'ah. Apply the same logic of definite vs indefinite.
This illustrates the general rule that specifying a unit binds the condition to its completion.
5
Apply to other conditional words like 'in a day' or 'in a month'
These also do not require completion because the condition is not attached to the full unit (the word 'in' indicates the action occurs within that time).
The passage explicitly includes this example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي : إن صُمْتِ يوماً فأنت طالق، تطلق حين غربت من يوم صامت ، بخلاف : إن صمت، فإنه يقع على صوم ساعة . [3] قوله : حين غربت ؛ أي يقعُ الطلاقُ عليها حين غروب الشمس يوم صومها، ونظيره : إن صمت ، صوماً فلا يقع إلا بتمام يوم ؛ لأنه مقدر بمعيار بخلاف : إن صمت ، فإنه يتعلّق الطلاق حينئذ بما يسمّى صوماً شرعاً، وقد وجد بركنه، وشرطه بإمساك ساعة فيقع به، وإن قطعه بعدها، وكذا : إن صمت في يوم أو في شهر ؛ لأنه لم يشترط كماله، وفي : إذا صليت صلاة فأنت طالق ، يقع الطلاق بركعتين، وفي : إذا صليت يقعُ بركعة. كذا في «الفتح»).
And in the phrase: 'If you fast a day, you are divorced,' she is divorced at sunset of the day she fasted. Unlike: 'if you fast,' — indeed it takes effect upon fasting an hour. [3] His saying: 'at sunset' means divorce occurs upon her at sunset of her day of fasting. Its analogy: 'if you fast, a fast' — it does not occur except with the completion of the day, because it is measured by a standard. Unlike: 'if you fast,' — then divorce is attached to what is called 'fasting' legally, and it has been found with its pillar and condition by refraining for an hour, so it occurs thereby, even if she breaks it afterwards. Similarly, 'if you fast in a day or in a month' — because he did not stipulate its completion. And in: 'if you pray a prayer, you are divorced' — divorce occurs with two rak'ahs. And in: 'if you pray' — it occurs with one rak'ah. As in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on conditional divorce related to fasting. It distinguishes between two phrases: (1) 'If you fast a day, you are divorced' — here, divorce occurs only at the end of the day (sunset), because the day is a complete unit measured by time. (2) 'If you fast' — here, divorce occurs as soon as she performs any action that legally counts as fasting, even for a moment (an hour), because the term is not qualified by a specific duration. The commentator draws an analogy with prayer: 'if you pray a prayer' requires two rak'ahs, while 'if you pray' requires only one rak'ah. This illustrates the Hanafi principle of interpreting conditional expressions according to their definite or indefinite form.

Key Terms
صوماً (sawman): Fasting as a legal act, which is fulfilled by abstaining from food, drink, and marital relations from dawn to sunset.
معيار (mi'yar): A standard or measure; here referring to the day as a complete unit of time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) At sunset of that day

Key Takeaway
The presence or absence of a definite time unit in a conditional expression determines whether the condition requires completion of that unit or can be fulfilled by the minimal legal act.

Conditional Divorce on Childbirth with Twins of Unknown Order
Rulings on conditional divorce when twins are born and the firstborn is unknown

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for a conditional divorce when twins are born with unknown order, distinguishing between qada and diyanah.

Core Idea
When twins are born and the firstborn is unknown, the court gives one divorce, but the husband must consider two as a precaution.

This lesson examines a nuanced case of conditional divorce (talaq ta'liqi) where the number of divorces depends on the gender of the child. When twins arrive and the order is unknown, the Hanafi school provides a dual ruling: one for the court (qada) and one for religious conscience (diyanah). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the law both externally and internally.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional divorce scenario
The husband says: 'You are divorced once if you give birth to a male, and twice if a female.' The wife gives birth to twins, but it is unknown which was delivered first.
The condition is linked to the firstborn.
2
Recognize the qada vs diyanah distinction
Qada is the court's ruling based on apparent certainty; diyanah is the religious duty based on what the individual knows or fears.
The judge rules only on what is certain.
3
Apply the Quranic evidence on iddah
Surah al-Talaq (65:4) states that the waiting period of a pregnant woman ends with delivery. Therefore, after the first delivery, the iddah ends, preventing any divorce from the second delivery.
The delivery is a condition for divorce, so it must occur before the iddah ends.
4
Determine the ruling for known vs unknown order
If the firstborn is known (male or female), the corresponding number of divorces takes effect, and the second birth does not add anything. If unknown, only one divorce is certain.
Certainty removes doubt.
5
Identify the precautionary diyanah ruling
The husband should treat the divorce as two (one more than the court ruling) to avoid undercount. This may affect later remarriage possibilities.
Tanzuh (scrupulousness) is recommended.

ولو علق طلقة بولادة ذكر وطلقتين بأنثى فولدتهما ، ولم يُدْرَ الأوّلُ طُلقت واحدةً قضاء وثنتين تَنَزُّهاً ) : أي ديانة : يعني فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى . وانقضت العدة بوضع الحمل ) : أي بالوضع الثاني ، وإنما لا يقع به طلاق آخر ؛ لأن العدة تنقضي بالوضع، قال الله : وَأَوَلَتُ الْأَعْمَالِ أَجَلُهُنَّ أَن يَضَعْنَ حَمَلَهُنَّ، ثُمَّ الوضع شرط الوقوع الطلاق ، فهو مؤخَّر عن الوضع، فتنقضي العدة بالوضع، فلا يقع بعده طلاق . فلا يخلو إما أن يعلم أولهما ولادة أو لم يعلم. فإن علم أنّ الأوّل كان ذكراً يقعُ ما علق به، ثم لا يقع شيء بتولّد الأنثى. وكذا إذا علم أن الأوّل كان أنثى يقعُ ما علّق به. فإن لم يدر الأول لزمه في القضاء طلاق واحد ؛ لأنه تيقن ، ووقوع الزائد مشكوك، والقاضي إنما يحكم بالمتيقن دون المشكوك ، وأمـا ديـانـة فينبغي أن يأخذ بالتطليقتين احتياطاً، حتى لو كان طلقها قبل ذلك واحدة، فلا ينبغي له أن يتزوجها حتى تنكح زوجاً غيره ؛ لاحتمال أنها مطلقة ثلاثاً. كذا في «النهاية».
And if he suspended one divorce upon the birth of a male and two divorces upon a female, and she gave birth to both, and it is not known which was first, she is divorced one divorce in judicial verdict (qada) and two divorces by way of scrupulousness (tanzuh), meaning diyanatan (conscientiously). The waiting period is completed by the delivery (of the child) i.e., by the second delivery. And indeed no other divorce occurs because the waiting period ends with delivery. Allah said: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they give birth to their offspring.' Then delivery is a condition for the occurrence of divorce, so it is later than delivery, so the waiting period ends with delivery, so no divorce occurs after it. So it is either that the first of them is known or not. If it is known that the first was a male, what was suspended falls, then nothing falls with the birth of the female. Likewise if it is known that the first was a female, what was suspended falls. If the first is not known, he is required in judicial verdict to issue one divorce because he is certain of it, and the occurrence of the extra is doubtful, and the judge only rules on what is certain, not the doubtful. But diyanatan (religiously), he should take the two divorces as a precaution, so that if he had divorced her once before, he should not marry her until she marries another husband, due to the possibility that she is thrice divorced. As in al-Nihayah.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Qur'an (Surah al-Talaq, verse 4) regarding a conditional divorce suspended on childbirth with a specific condition: one talaq if the child is male, two if female. When twins are born, the order matters. If the firstborn is known, the corresponding number of divorces takes effect, and the second birth does not trigger additional divorce because the waiting period ends with the first delivery? Actually, the text clarifies: when the first delivery occurs, waiting period begins (for a pregnant woman, it ends with delivery). So if the firstborn is male, one divorce occurs, waiting period is ongoing with the second fetus, but when the second is delivered, the waiting period ends, and since divorce is conditional on delivery, it cannot occur after the period ends. Hence no additional divorce. If the first is female, two divorces occur. If the order is unknown, in judicial ruling only one divorce is given because certainty is limited to one. Religiously (diyanatan), the husband should consider himself as having given two divorces as a precaution, especially if he had previously given one divorce, making it possible that three occurred, thus she becomes prohibited until she marries another.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): Judicial ruling based on apparent evidence; here it means the court issues one divorce because certainty is limited to one.
تَنَزُّهاً (tanzuhan): By way of scrupulousness/precaution; here it means the husband should consider himself as having given two divorces (diyanatan) to avoid the possibility of undercounting.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before remarrying. For a pregnant woman, it ends with delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Three divorces
B) Two divorces
C) One divorce
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
When the order of twins is unknown in a conditional divorce, the court rules one divorce with certainty, but religious precaution requires treating it as two.

Expiration of 'Iddah and Suspended Divorce: Sequential Births
Ruling on sequential births in suspended divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on suspended divorce when a woman gives birth to twins sequentially and apply the distinction between judicial ruling (qada) and personal piety (diyanah).

Core Idea
Only the first birth triggers a suspended divorce; the second birth ends the iddah and blocks any further divorce.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies a nuanced scenario in suspended divorce (al-ḥilf bi-al-ṭalāq). When a husband conditions divorce on the gender of a child, and twins are born one after another, only the first birth activates the divorce. The second birth, though also a birth, ends the iddah period and thus cannot produce any additional divorce. The text also addresses the uncertainty when the gender of the first-born is unknown, drawing a critical distinction between what a judge can enforce (qada) and what the husband should observe in his conscience (diyanah). This ruling is grounded in the Quranic verse (65:4) that defines the iddah of a pregnant woman as delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario of suspended divorce
The husband suspends divorce on the birth of a child: if the child is male, one divorce; if female, two divorces. The woman gives birth to twins sequentially (one after the other).
The first birth is the condition for the suspended divorce.
2
Apply the rule: only the first birth triggers divorce
The first birth activates the corresponding divorce (1 or 2 depending on gender). The second birth does not cause any additional divorce because the iddah from the first divorce expires with the second birth.
The Quranic proof (65:4) states that the iddah for a pregnant woman ends with delivery.
3
Analyze the scenario when the gender of the first child is unknown
If it is unknown whether the first-born was male or female, the judge (qada) enforces only one divorce, because that is the minimum certain amount. The extra divorce is doubtful, and a judge rules only on certainty.
The husband may have incurred two divorces in reality, but the court cannot enforce doubt.
4
Distinguish between judicial ruling and personal piety
In private (diyanatan), the husband should consider himself bound by two divorces as a precaution. If he had previously divorced her once, he should not remarry her until she marries another, to avoid the possibility of three divorces.
This precautionary ruling is from al-Nihayah, reflecting the later Hanafi fatwa practice.

The Book of Divorce / Oaths by Divorce and Exceptions. And the 'iddah expires with the birth of the child. ( ) [2] 325. And the 'iddah expires with the birth of the child: meaning with the second birth. And it is that no other divorce occurs by it because the 'iddah expires with the birth. Allah said: 'And those who are pregnant, their term is that they give birth to their child.' Then birth is a condition for the occurrence of divorce, so it is postponed after birth. Thus the 'iddah expires with the birth, so no divorce occurs after it. So it is not free from either knowing which of the two is born first or not knowing. If it is known that the first was a male, what was suspended by it occurs, then nothing occurs by the birth of the female. Likewise, if it is known that the first was a female, what was suspended by it occurs. If the first is not known, then in judicial proceedings one divorce is binding because he is certain, and the occurrence of the extra is doubtful, and the judge only rules on what is certain, not on what is doubtful. As for religious piety, he should take two divorces as a precaution, such that if he had divorced her once before that, he should not marry her until she marries another husband, because of the possibility that she is divorced thrice. Thus in al-Nihayah. [1] His statement: 'the 'iddah' means the obligatory one due to the occurrence of divorce by the first birth, whether male or female. [2] His statement: 'and it is that no other divorce occurs...' etc., is a rebuttal of an anticipated objection. The objection: since he suspended one divorce on the birth of a male and two on the birth of a female, and she gave birth to one after another, and by the first birth what was suspended occurred, then by the second birth what was suspended should also occur, so three would occur, as occurs in the case of simultaneous birth. The summary of the rebuttal: when what was suspended by the first birth occurred, she became a woman in 'iddah, for the 'iddah is from the time of divorce, and she is pregnant with the second as she has not yet given birth to it, and the 'iddah of a pregnant woman is the birth of the child by textual evidence. So at the second birth, the 'iddah expires, and there is no divorce after the 'iddah, unlike if she gave birth to both together, for in that case the 'iddah is by periods. [3] His statement: 'And those who are pregnant' (wa ulatu al-ahmal) — with fathah — plural of haml, meaning those with pregnancy when they are divorced, their term (ajaluhunna) — with fathah on hamzah and jim — meaning their 'iddah is that they give birth to their pregnancy, because the purpose of legislating 'iddah is to clear the womb, and that is achieved by birth. [4] His statement: 'condition'—for he suspended divorce on the birth of the child, so birth is a condition, and a condition precedes the consequence, not simultaneous, so the occurrence of divorce must be after birth, and after the second birth there remains no subject for its occurrence because her 'iddah expires by it, meaning concurrent with it. And this is the meaning of their statement: 'Divorce concurrent with the expiration of 'iddah does not occur.'
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof on a specific scenario in suspended divorce. The author begins by establishing a general rule: when a man suspends divorce on the birth of a child (e.g., 'if you give birth to a male, you are divorced once; if a female, twice'), and the woman gives birth to twins one after the other, the first birth triggers the suspended divorce. The second birth does not trigger any additional divorce because the 'iddah (waiting period) from the first divorce expires with the birth of the second child. The author cites the Quranic verse (65:4) as proof that the 'iddah of a pregnant woman ends with delivery. The ruling then differentiates scenarios: if the gender of the first child is known, the corresponding suspended divorce occurs. If unknown, in a court of law only one divorce is enforced (due to certainty), but the husband should consider himself bound by two divorces in his conscience (diyanatan) as a precaution, and if he had already divorced her once, he should not remarry her until she marries another (to avoid possibility of three divorces). The footnotes clarify the reasoning: the condition (birth) precedes the consequence (divorce), so divorce occurs after birth. After the second birth, the 'iddah ends, and there can be no further divorce. This is a classic Hanafi application of textual proof and precautionary principle.

Key Terms
العدة (al-ʿiddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before remarrying.
وضع الحمل (waḍʿ al-ḥaml): The act of giving birth to a child.
الحلف بالطلاق (al-ḥilf bi-al-ṭalāq): An oath using divorce as a condition, e.g., 'if you do X, you are divorced.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
When a suspended divorce is conditioned on childbirth and twins are born sequentially, only the first birth triggers divorce; the second birth ends the 'iddah, and any subsequent divorce cannot occur.

Suspending Divorce on Two Conditions: Repeated 'If' with and without Conjunction
Expansion on rules for repeated conditions in suspended divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for determining when a suspended divorce occurs when a husband repeats the conditional particle 'if' with two conditions, distinguishing between cases with conjunction (ʿaṭf) and without, and considering the position of the consequence.

Core Idea
When 'if' is repeated without 'and', both conditions must be fulfilled in reversed order; with 'and', one condition suffices unless the consequence is delayed.

In Hanafi law, the wording of a conditional divorce determines its occurrence. This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah expands on the rules when a husband suspends divorce on two conditions using the particle 'if' (in) either with or without the conjunction 'and' (wa). It explains how the placement of the consequence (jazāʾ) and the customary order of conditions affect the outcome. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying conditional divorce clauses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
The passage distinguishes between repeating 'if' with the conjunction 'and' (ʿaṭf) and repeating it without conjunction. Your task is to spot these two scenarios in the Arabic text and note the key difference in how the conditions are treated.
The conjunction 'and' (عطف) connects two conditions with wa, while repetition without is just in...in...
2
Analyze the case with conjunction and delayed consequence
When the consequence is delayed after both conditions joined by 'and', the divorce depends on both conditions being fulfilled. Trace the author's reasoning: the consequence is attached to both because the second condition has no independent ruling.
Example: 'If Zaid comes and if Zaid comes, you are divorced.' Divorce occurs only when both events happen.
3
Analyze the case without conjunction
Without 'and', the implied order is reversed: the second condition is treated as preceding the first. Divorce requires both conditions in that implied order. Examine how the custom (ʿurf) influences whether the order is reversed.
Example: 'If you eat, if you wear, you are divorced.' The implied order is wear then eat.
4
Consider the position of the consequence
The passage discusses three positions of the consequence: advanced (before both conditions), intermediate (between them), and delayed (after both). The requirement of the marriage bond (milk) at the time of each condition varies accordingly. Your task is to map the rule for each position.
If consequence is advanced or delayed, marriage bond needed at last condition only; if intermediate, needed at both.
5
Summarize the overall rule
Synthesize: with repetition without conjunction, occurrence depends on both conditions; with conjunction, occurrence depends on one if consequence is advanced or intermediate, but on both if delayed. Note the exception for customarily dependent conditions.
The passage ends with 'if he does not repeat the conditional particle, then...' – this is incomplete, but the principles covered are sufficient.

وكذا : إن دعوتني إن أجبتك، أو : إن ركبت الدابة إن أتيتني ؛ لأنهما إذا كانا مرتبين عرفاً أضمرت كلمة ثم. وكذا إذا توسط الجزاء ؛ لأنه لما تخلّل الجزاء بين الشرطين بحرف الوصل، وهو الفاء كان الأوّل شرطاً لانعقاد اليمين، والثاني شرط الحنث، كإن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق إن كلّمت فلاناً، ويشترط حينئذ قيام الملكِ عند الشرط الأول.

And if he suspends divorce... (text of commentary) 'And if he suspends divorce with him, and the condition precedes the consequence, not simultaneous.' So the occurrence of divorce must be after birth, and after the second birth there is no subject for its occurrence because her 'iddah expires by it, meaning concurrent with it. And this is the meaning of their statement: 'Divorce concurrent with the expiration of 'iddah does not occur.' [1] His statement: 'And if he suspends divorce' means by repeating the condition, such as conjoining one condition to another and delaying the consequence, like: 'If Zaid comes and if Zaid comes, you are divorced.' So divorce does not occur until both come, because he conjoined a pure condition to a condition with no ruling, then mentioned the consequence, so it is attached to both; it occurs only with both. If he intended occurrence by one of them, his intention is valid by presenting the consequence to one of them; and there is strictness in it. Or by repeating the conditional particle without conjunction, like: 'If you eat, if you wear, you are divorced.' So she is not divorced until she wears then eats. So the implied meaning is: 'If you wear, then if you eat, you are divorced.' And likewise: 'Every woman I marry, if I speak to so-and-so, she is divorced.' The later is advanced so the implied meaning becomes: 'If I speak to so-and-so, every woman I marry is divorced.' This is when the second condition is not customarily dependent on the first, and the consequence is after both conditions or before them. Otherwise, each condition is in its place, like: 'If you eat, if you drink, you are divorced,' so that if she drinks then eats, she is not divorced. And likewise: 'If you invite me, if I accept,' or 'If you ride the animal, if you come to me,' because when they are customarily dependent, the word 'then' is implied. And likewise if the consequence intervenes, because when the consequence intervenes between the two conditions with the conjunctive particle which is 'fa', then the first is a condition for the formation of the oath, and the second is a condition for the violation, like: 'If you enter the house, then you are divorced, if you speak to so-and-so.' And at that time, the existence of the marriage bond is required at the first condition. The conclusion: if he repeats the conditional particle without conjunction, occurrence depends on both of them, but if he advances the consequence over them or delays it, the marriage bond is required at the last one which is the uttered one, or not upon advancement and delay; and if he places it between them, the marriage bond is required at both. If it is with conjunction, it depends on one of them if he advances the consequence or places it between; if he delays it, it depends on both. If he does not repeat the conditional particle, then...

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-reasoning on the rules of suspending divorce with multiple conditions. The author begins by clarifying the meaning of 'suspending divorce' with repeated conditions. There are two main scenarios: (1) When the conditions are conjoined with 'and' (عطف), e.g., 'If Zaid comes and if Zaid comes, you are divorced.' Here, divorce occurs only when both conditions are fulfilled, because the consequence is attached to both. If the husband intended divorce by just one, his intention is valid but strict (i.e., he is considered to have intended a less likely meaning). (2) When the conditional particle is repeated without conjunction, e.g., 'If you eat, if you wear, you are divorced.' Here, the implied meaning is that the second condition is the real condition, so divorce occurs only after the second condition is met, but this depends on the customary order. The passage then discusses various sub-cases: when the consequence is placed before, between, or after the conditions; when the conditions are customarily dependent; and when the consequence intervenes with 'fa'. The conclusion summarises: with repetition without conjunction, occurrence depends on both conditions, but the requirement of the marriage bond at the time of each condition varies. This is a detailed Hanafi analysis of conditional language in divorce, showing how the school uses linguistic principles and legal reasoning to determine outcomes.

Key Terms
تعليق الطلاق (taʿlīq al-ṭalāq): Suspending the occurrence of divorce upon a future condition.
أداة الشرط (adāt al-sharṭ): The conditional particle, such as 'in' or 'idha', used to introduce a condition.
عطف (ʿaṭf): Conjunction, i.e., connecting two conditions with 'wa' (and).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife: 'If you eat, if you wear, you are divorced.' She wears a dress, then eats. Does divorce occur according to the Hanafi rule?
A) Yes, because both conditions are met in the required order.
B) Yes, because wearing alone suffices.
C) No, because the order is wrong: she must eat first then wear.
D) No, because the conditions are contradictory.

Key Takeaway
The occurrence of a suspended divorce with repeated conditional particles depends on whether the conditions are joined by 'and', the position of the consequence, and the customary order of events.

Conditional Divorce with Two Conditions: Abu Hanifa vs. Zufar
Explaining the ruling on conditional divorce with two conditions and the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Abu Hanifa's rule that only the second condition need occur within the marriage for the conditional divorce to take effect, and explain why Zufar disagrees.

Core Idea
In conditional divorce with two actions, only the second action's timing within the marriage matters.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in conditional divorce (ta'liq al-talaq) where the husband's condition involves two actions. The key dispute between Abu Hanifa and his companion Zufar revolves around whether both actions must occur during the same marriage or only the second. Through the example from al-Hidayah, you will learn how to analyze such conditions and identify the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two-part condition
Recognize that the husband's statement contains two conditions (e.g., speaking to two specific people). The divorce is suspended on the occurrence of both.
The condition is typically phrased with 'and' (waw), indicating both must happen, but does not specify that they must happen in the same marriage.
2
Determine the temporal sequence
Map out when each action occurs relative to the marriage timeline. In the classic case, the first action occurs after a prior divorce (during the `iddah or after), then the couple remarries, and the second action occurs in the new marriage.
The first action is 'outside possession' (ghayr al-milk) because the wife was not married at that time.
3
Apply Abu Hanifa's rule
According to Abu Hanifa, the divorce occurs because the second condition was fulfilled while the wife was in the husband's possession (inside the marriage). The first condition's timing is irrelevant; only the second must be within the marriage.
The passage states: 'If the second is found in possession, it occurs.'
4
Understand Zufar's objection
Zufar argues that both conditions must occur in the same continuous marriage. Since the first condition occurred outside the marriage, the entire conditional pronouncement is not fulfilled, and no divorce occurs.
Zufar's reasoning is based on the idea that the condition is a single integrated event.
5
Apply to all four scenarios
The passage enumerates four possible combinations: both in marriage (divorce occurs), both outside (no divorce), first in marriage/second outside (no divorce, because second is outside), and first outside/second in marriage (the disputed case).
Only the fourth scenario is disputed; the other three are agreed upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بشيئين يقع إن وجد الثاني في الملك وإلا فلا. فقولُه : إن وجد الثاني في الملك : يشمل ما إذا وجدا في الملك، أو وجد الثاني فقط في الملك، وقوله : وإلا فلا : يشمل ما إذا لم يوجد شيء منهما في الملك، أو وُجِدَ الأول في الملك دون الثاني. قال في «الهداية»: إن قال لها : إن كلّمت أبا عمرو وأبا يوسف فأنت طالق ثلاثاً، ثم طلّقها واحدة فبانت وانقضت عدتها فكلّمت أبا عمرو. ثم تزوجها فكلّمت أبا يوسف فهي طالق ثلاثاً مع الواحدة الأولى. وقال زفر : لا يقع. وهذه على وجوه : أما إن وجد الشرطان في الملك فيقع، وهذا ظاهر، أو وجدا في غير الملك فلا يقع، أو وجد الأول في الملك والثاني في غير الملك فلا يقع أيضاً؛ لأن الجزاء لا ينزل في غير الملك فلا يقع، أو وجد الأول في غير الملك والثاني في الملك، وهي مسألة الكتاب الخلافية.
With two things, it occurs if the second is found in possession, otherwise not. His statement: 'if the second is found in possession' includes when both are found in possession, or only the second is found in possession. And his statement: 'otherwise not' includes when neither is found in possession, or the first is found in possession but not the second. He said in al-Hidayah: If he said to her, 'If you speak to Abu Amr and Abu Yusuf, you are thrice divorced,' then he divorced her once (irrevocably) and her iddah expired, so she spoke to Abu Amr. Then he married her again, then she spoke to Abu Yusuf—she is thrice divorced in addition to the first. Zufar said: It does not occur. And this has aspects: If both conditions occur in possession, it occurs—this is apparent. If both occur outside possession, it does not occur. If the first occurs in possession and the second outside possession, it does not occur either, because the consequence does not occur outside possession. If the first occurs outside possession and the second in possession—this is the disputed issue of the book.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding conditional divorce. The matn states that when a divorce is conditional on two things (e.g., speaking to two people), the divorce occurs only if the second condition is fulfilled while the wife is still legally the husband's wife (in his possession). The commentary elaborates that 'in possession' means during the marriage. If both conditions occur while she is in the marriage, the divorce definitely occurs. If only the second occurs in the marriage but the first occurred prior (when she was not his wife, e.g., after a divorce but before remarriage), the divorce still occurs according to Abu Hanifa, as in the example from al-Hidayah: after one irrevocable divorce and remarriage, if the first condition was met before remarriage and the second after, she is divorced. Imam Zufar disagreed, holding that both conditions must occur during one continuous marriage for the conditional divorce to take effect. The passage then lists four possible scenarios: both in marriage (divorce occurs), both outside (no divorce), first in marriage but second outside (no divorce, since the consequence cannot occur outside marriage), and first outside but second in marriage (the disputed case, where Abu Hanifa says divorce occurs and Zufar says no).

Key Terms
الملك (al-milk): Possession; in the context of marriage, it means the state of being legally married and having conjugal rights. The wife is said to be 'in the possession' of the husband as long as the marriage contract is intact.
الشرط (al-shart): Condition; a stipulation that suspends the occurrence of the divorce on its fulfillment. The divorce is not effective until the condition is met.
الجزاء (al-jaza'): Consequence; the legal effect of the condition. In this context, the 'jaza'' is the divorce (the three talaqs) that is suspended upon the condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife: 'If you speak to Abu Amr and Abu Yusuf, you are thrice divorced.' He then divorces her once (irrevocably), she completes her iddah, and then she speaks to Abu Amr. Later, he remarries her, and she speaks to Abu Yusuf. According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling?
A) She is not divorced because the first condition occurred outside the marriage.
B) She is thrice divorced in addition to the first divorce (total four).
C) She is divorced once (the first) but not thrice.
D) The condition is invalid because it was not fulfilled within one continuous marriage.

Key Takeaway
In conditional divorce with two conditions, the relied-upon Hanafi position is that only the second condition must occur within the marriage for the divorce to take effect.

Immediate Divorce Nullifies Conditional Divorce
Explaining how immediate divorce (tanjiz) nullifies prior conditional divorce in certain cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that immediate triple divorce nullifies prior conditional divorce and apply it to scenarios involving tahlil.

Core Idea
Immediate triple divorce extinguishes the conditional divorce completely, even after a new marriage.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains a critical nuance in Hanafi divorce law: if a conditional divorce (such as 'if you enter the house, you are divorced thrice') is followed by an immediate triple divorce, the condition becomes void. Even if the wife later returns through tahlil, the condition has no effect. The logic rests on the complete severance of the original marriage bond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is a conditional divorce (ta'liq) followed by an immediate triple divorce (tanjiz). The ruling is that the conditional divorce is nullified (batil).
The nullification depends on the immediate divorce being a complete one (triple for free woman, double for slave).
2
Understand the Scope of 'Immediate'
Not every immediate divorce nullifies a conditional one. Only a divorce that removes lawfulness fully (hill)—i.e., triple for a free woman, double for a slave—has this effect.
If the immediate divorce is less than that (e.g., one single divorce), the conditional divorce remains valid.
3
Trace the Rationale (Talil)
The reason (illa) is that the conditional divorce is tied to the original marriage bond. Once that bond is completely severed by a triple talaq, a new marriage (even after tahlil) creates a fresh legal relationship. The old conditional divorce cannot attach to the new marriage.
This is based on the principle that a condition cannot outlive its underlying contract.
4
Apply to a Scenario
Husband says: 'If you visit your mother, you are thrice divorced.' He then pronounces an immediate triple divorce. She undergoes tahlil and remarries him. Later she visits her mother. No divorce occurs because the conditional was nullified.
The condition, even though fulfilled after the new marriage, triggers no effect.
5
Note the Exception
If the immediate divorce is less than a full talaq (e.g., one divorce), the conditional divorce is not nullified. The condition can still take effect in the same marriage or a new marriage.
This is because the marriage bond is not completely severed; only partial divorce occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتنجيز يُبطل التعليق، فلو علق الثلاث بشرط، ثم نجز الثلاث، ثم عادت إليه بعد التحليل، ثم وجد الشرط لا يقع شيء. قوله : والتنجيز ؛ هو مقابل التعليق، بمعنى إيقاع الطلاق حالاً، وليس المراد به مطلق التنجيز بل فرد منه، وتفصيله على ما في «الهداية» وشروحها : إن تنجيز الثلاث للحرّة، والثنتين في الأمة يبطل تعليقه بالثلاث فما دونها، وبالجملة: التعليق يبطل بزوال الحل بوقوع الثلاث؛ لحدوث ملك جديد بعده، فلا يقع فيه المعلق، وتنجيز ما دون الثلاث لا يبطل تعليقه.
And immediate (divorce) nullifies conditional (divorce). So if he conditioned triple (divorce) on a condition, then he made the triple immediate, then she returned to him after making lawful (tahlil), then the condition was found, nothing occurs. His statement: 'immediate' is opposite of conditional, meaning effecting divorce immediately. Not every immediate (divorce) is meant, but a specific type. Its detail according to al-Hidayah and its commentaries: That immediate triple divorce for a free woman, and double for a slave woman, nullifies his condition concerning triple and less. In summary: The conditional is nullified by the removal of lawfulness (hill) due to the occurrence of triple (divorce), due to a new marriage occurring after it, so the conditional does not occur in it. And immediate divorce of less than three does not nullify his condition.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage explains a key principle in Hanafi law: if a person conditionally divorces his wife (e.g., saying 'if you enter the house, you are thrice divorced'), and then before the condition is met, he pronounces an immediate triple divorce, the conditional divorce is nullified. The reason is that the immediate triple divorce renders the wife unlawful (haram) for him, and even if she later becomes lawful again through tahlil (marrying another, that marriage ending, and returning to the first husband), the conditional divorce is not revived. The condition, if it occurs after the new marriage, does not trigger the divorce. The commentary clarifies that 'immediate' here specifically means a divorce that removes lawfulness fully (triple for free, double for slave). If the immediate divorce is less than that (e.g., one or two single divorces), it does not nullify the conditional. The underlying reason is that the conditional divorce was tied to the original marriage bond; once that bond is completely severed by a triple talaq and a new marriage occurs, the condition is considered to have lapsed. This is because the 'hill' (permissibility of the wife) ended, and a new marriage creates a new legal relationship independent of the old condition.

Key Terms
التنجيز (al-tanjiz): Immediate divorce; pronouncing a divorce without any condition, effective instantly. In this passage, it specifically refers to a divorce that removes the lawfulness (hill) completely (triple for free woman, double for slave).
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Conditional divorce; suspending the divorce on the fulfillment of a condition. It is valid and binding upon fulfillment.
التحليل (al-tahlil): Making lawful; specifically the process by which a woman who has been thrice divorced becomes lawful again for her first husband. This requires her to marry another man, consummate the marriage, and then be divorced or widowed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife: 'If you visit your mother, you are thrice divorced.' Before she visits, he pronounces an immediate triple divorce. She then goes through tahlil and remarries him. Later, she visits her mother. What is the ruling according to the text?
A) She is thrice divorced because the condition is fulfilled.
B) She is not divorced because the immediate divorce nullified the conditional divorce.
C) She is divorced once due to the immediate divorce, but the condition does not apply.
D) The condition is valid and she is divorced thrice from the new marriage.

Key Takeaway
Immediate triple divorce extinguishes a prior conditional triple divorce, even if the condition is fulfilled after a subsequent marriage.

Conditional Triple Talaq and the Rule of 'Uqr
Explaining the ruling of conditional triple talaq on intercourse, definition of ilaj, and rule of 'uqr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling regarding 'uqr when conditional triple talaq is triggered by intercourse and distinguish between remaining and reinsertion.

Core Idea
In conditional triple talaq, only withdrawal followed by reinsertion after talaq triggers 'uqr, not mere remaining.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains a specific case of conditional triple talaq. When a husband conditions triple talaq on intercourse, the talaq occurs immediately upon insertion (ilaj). The key question is whether any subsequent acts require financial compensation ('uqr). By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the moment of talaq
The husband suspends triple talaq on intercourse. Insertion (ilaj) triggers the talaq instantly. Understand that the wife becomes a forbidden stranger (ajnabiyyah muharramah) at that moment.
Talaq occurs even before withdrawal.
2
Define ilaj legally
Ilaj is defined as the meeting of the circumcised parts (the glans fully inside). This is the Hanafi definition of full intercourse (jima'). Note that remaining (labth) is not considered a separate act.
The text uses kinayah: 'the two circumcised parts meet'.
3
Distinguish between remaining and reinsertion
If the husband remains inserted after talaq, no 'uqr is due because only the original insertion counts. But if he withdraws and then reinserts, that is a new act of intercourse with a now-forbidden woman, requiring 'uqr.
Remaining is not jima'; only insertion is.
4
Apply the rule of 'uqr and note raj'ah
'Uqr is wajib upon withdrawal and reinsertion, calculated as mahr al-mithl (dower of her like in beauty). Additionally, if the original divorce was revocable, the reinsertion counts as raj'ah (return) because it is intercourse with the divorced wife during iddah.
Hadd penalties are not applied due to shubhah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن عَلق الثلاث بوطء زوجته فأولج، أي أدخل حشفته حتى التقى الختانان، ولبث فلا عُقر عليه. العُقر: مهر المثل. وقيل: هو مقدار أجرة الوطء لو كان الزنا حلالاً. وكذا لو علق عتق أمته بوطئها، ولم يصر مراجعاً به في الرجعي، فلو نزع ثم أولج يجب العقر، وكان رجعة. قوله: التقى الختانان: أي ختان الرجل والمرأة – وهو بالكسر – موضع القطع عند الختان، وهو كناية عن إدخال الذكر في القبل. قوله: فلا عقر عليه: يعني إذا أدخل ذكره في فرجها ومكث وقع الطلاق بمجرد الإيلاج، فصارت أجنبية محرمة، ثم مكث بعد ذلك لا يوجب العقر، بناءً على أن الوطء لا يخلو عن مهر أو عقر؛ وذلك لأن اللبث ليس بجماع، فإن الجماع هو الإيلاج، والموجب للعقر إنما هو الجماع، فإن أخرج ثم أولج يجب العقر، ويصير الوطء رجعة، ومجرد المكث لا يعد رجعة. قوله: مهر المثل: قال في «العناية»: «قال في «ديوان الأدب»: هو مهر المثل إذا وطئت بشبهة». وفي «الفتح»: «العقر: هو مهر مثلها في الجمال؛ أي ما يرغب به في مثلها جمالاً فقط، وأما ما قيل: ما يستأجر به مثلها للزنا لو جاز فليس معناه، بل العادة أن ما يعطى لذلك أقل مما يعطى مهراً؛ لأن الثاني للبقاء، بخلاف الأول». قوله: فلو نزع: أي لو أخرج الذكر ثم أدخل يجب العقر، ويصير رجعة إن كان الطلاق المعلق رجعياً؛ لوجود وطء المطلقة، ولا يقام الحد؛ لأن الحد يسقط بالشبهات كما سيأتي إن شاء الله في موضعه.
And whoever conditions triple (talaq) on intercourse with his wife, then he inserted his glans until the two circumcised parts meet, and he remained, so no 'uqr is due on him. 'Uqr: the dower of the like (mahr al-mithl). And it is said: it is the amount of the hire of intercourse if zina were permissible. Similarly, if he conditioned the freeing of his slave woman on his intercourse with her, and he did not become a returner (muraji') by it in the revocable (divorce), so if he withdrew then inserted, the 'uqr becomes obligatory, and it becomes a return (raj'ah). His statement: the two circumcised parts meet – meaning the circumcision area of the man and woman – with a kasrah, the place of cutting at circumcision, and it is a kinayah for inserting the male organ into the vagina. His statement: so no 'uqr is due on him – meaning if he inserted his organ into her vagina and remained, the divorce occurs merely by insertion, so she becomes a forbidden foreigner, then remaining thereafter does not necessitate the 'uqr, based on the fact that intercourse is not free from a dower or 'uqr; that is because staying (labth) is not intercourse, for intercourse is insertion, and that which necessitates 'uqr is indeed intercourse. So if he withdraws and then inserts, the 'uqr becomes obligatory, and that intercourse becomes a return, and mere remaining does not count as a return. His statement: mahr al-mithl – he said in al-'Inayah: 'He said in Diwan al-Adab: it is the dower of the like if a woman is had intercourse with by mistake (shubhah).' And in al-Fath: 'al-'uqr is the dower of her like in beauty; that is, what is desired in her like in beauty only. As for what is said: what would be hired for her like for zina if it were permissible – that is not its meaning; rather, customarily what is given for that is less than what is given as dower because the second is for permanence, contrary to the first.' His statement: 'so if he withdrew' – meaning if he removes the male organ then inserts, the 'uqr becomes obligatory, and it becomes a return if the conditional divorce was revocable; because of the existence of intercourse with the divorcee, and the hadd penalty is not established because hadd is dropped by shubuhat, as will come, God willing, in its place.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific case: a husband conditions triple talaq on intercourse with his wife. The moment he inserts the glans (defined as the meeting of the circumcised parts), the triple talaq takes effect immediately, even before withdrawal. Upon the talaq, the wife becomes a 'forbidden foreigner' (ajnabiyyah muharramah), meaning she is no longer his wife. If he then remains inserted (labtha) after the talaq, he does not owe her a dower (sadaq) or 'uqr (compensation for dubious intercourse) because the only intercourse that occurred was the one that triggered the talaq; remaining is not considered a separate act of intercourse that would require compensation. However, if he withdraws and then reinserts, that second insertion is a new act of intercourse with a woman who is now a stranger (haram), and this is considered dubious intercourse, for which he must pay 'uqr (compensation equal to mahr al-mithl). Additionally, if the original conditional divorce was revocable (i.e., less than triple), the second insertion would also count as a return (raj'ah) because it is intercourse with his divorced wife during the iddah. The passage also clarifies that the definition of insertion (ilaj) is the meeting of the circumcised parts, which is a specific anatomical description. The 'uqr is defined as mahr al-mithl (the dower appropriate for a woman of her beauty) rather than an estimated hire for zina, because the latter assumes permissibility and is not analogous. The commentary from al-Fath distinguishes between the two: the dower is for permanence, while a hypothetical hire for zina would be temporary and less.

Key Terms
الإيلاج (al-ilaj): Insertion of the male glans (hashafah) into the female vagina until the circumcised parts meet (i.e., the glans is fully inside). It is a kinayah for sexual intercourse.
العقر (al-'uqr): Compensation paid by a man who has intercourse with a woman in a situation of legal uncertainty (shubhah) or without a valid marriage contract. It is akin to mahr al-mithl (dower of the like), calculated based on the woman's beauty.
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): Return; the act by which a husband takes back his wife during the iddah after a revocable divorce (talaq raj’i). This can be done by word or by certain actions, including intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man conditionally divorces his wife three times on the condition that he has intercourse with her. He inserts and remains inserted after the talaq takes effect. Then he withdraws and reinserts a second time. What is the ruling regarding 'uqr?
A) 'Uqr is due for the first insertion but not the second.
B) 'Uqr is due for the second insertion but not the first.
C) No 'uqr is due at all because the divorce was conditional.
D) 'Uqr is due for both insertions.

Key Takeaway
In conditional triple talaq, only a new act of insertion after withdrawal triggers 'uqr; mere remaining does not.

8 min
The Legal Effect of 'If Allah Wills' in Divorce: Ta'liq or Ibtal?
A ruling on divorce with exception and the intra-Hanafi dispute on its legal nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ta'liq and ibtal interpretations of the divorce exception 'إن شاء الله' and identify the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf) of the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'You are divorced if Allah wills' with immediate connection, the divorce does not occur, but the legal nature divides the Hanafi school into ta'liq and ibtal.

The simple addition of 'if Allah wills' to a divorce pronouncement triggers a detailed analysis. The Hanafi school agrees that if the exception is connected, the divorce does not take effect. However, the underlying reasoning splits into two camps: suspension (ta'liq) and nullification (ibtal). This lesson unpacks the evidence, the dispute between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, and the practical differences that emerge in scenarios like death or precedence. Understanding this dispute is essential for correctly applying the ruling to real cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling and its proof
From the matn, when the husband says 'You are divorced if Allah wills' with connection, the divorce does not occur. The hadith about oaths provides the analogy. State the ruling and the proof.
The hadith is: 'Whoever takes an oath and says: If Allah wills, there is no breaking of oath upon him.'
2
Understand the condition of connection
The exception must be immediately connected. A long pause without necessity, or idle talk, breaks the connection. However, pauses for emphasis or calling are acceptable. Recognise what counts as a break.
Examples of breaks: silence, coughing, sneezing, belching. Examples of acceptable: repetition for emphasis ('You are divorced, divorced if Allah wills'), calling ('O so-and-so').
3
Present the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf views the exception as ta'liq (suspension) on the condition of Allah's will; Muhammad views it as ibtal (nullification) of the entire statement. The proof for each view is based on the general principle of istithna.
There is also a reversed narration of who held which view.
4
Analyse the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence appears in two scenarios: (1) If 'if Allah wills' precedes the divorce pronouncement, under ta'liq the divorce occurs (no condition particle), under ibtal it does not. (2) The statement counts as an oath only under ta'liq; under ibtal, it is not an oath.
This affects whether the husband must expiate for breaking an oath.
5
Apply to the death scenarios
If the wife dies before the exception is completed, the divorce does not occur because death prevents the creation of obligation. If the husband dies, the divorce occurs because the exception was not connected, and the divorce stands. Understand the rationale.
The difference is that death of the speaker (husband) does not cancel the divorce if he intended the exception but didn't utter it; death of the recipient (wife) voids the obligation.

Source Text

ولو قال : أنتِ طالق إن شاءَ اللهُ تعالى متصلاً ، أو ماتت قبل قوله : إن شاء الله تعالى لم يقع، ولو مات هو يقع
[١] قوله : متصلا ; إنما شرط الاتصال لكونه تعليقاً صورة، فلا يكون تعليقاً عند [؟]، وهو بأن يوجد بين اللفظين فاصل كثير من سكوت بلا ضرورة تنفّس ونحوه: كالسعال والجشاء والعطاس ونحو ذلك ، ويضر الفصل أيضاً بالكلام اللغو لا بما يفيد التأكيد نحو : أنت طالق طالق إن شاء الله ، وللنداء نحو : أنت طالق يا فلانة إن شاء الله. كذا في «الفتح» و«البحر».
