Inheritance in Revocable Divorce: The iddah Preserves the Marriage
Ruling and dispute on inheritance during revocable divorce iddah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inheritance for a revocably divorced woman whose husband dies during her iddah, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A revocably divorced woman inherits if her husband dies during iddah because the marriage partially persists.

The classic question: does a divorced woman inherit from her ex-husband if he dies while she is still in her waiting period? The Hanafi school says yes, because revocable divorce does not completely sever the marriage. This lesson examines the ruling, its proof, and the disagreement with the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify the ruling: a woman in revocable divorce inherits if her husband dies during her iddah, regardless of why the divorce occurred.
The text says 'in all circumstances' (fi al-ahwal ajma').
2
Identify the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school argues she does not inherit because marriage ends at death; inheritance requires a cause at death.
They also argue after iddah by consensus she does not inherit.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof: during iddah, some marital rulings remain (e.g., cannot marry her sister, can take her back), so inheritance remains as well.
This is based on the partial persistence of marriage.
4
Examine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in Hanafi, she inherits; in Shafi'i, she does not. Also, if iddah expires before death, both agree she does not inherit.
Consensus after iddah: 'la tarith ijma'an'.

(٤) قوله : لا ترث إجماعاً ؛ أي بيننا وبين الشافعي له وغيره، وذلك لانقطاع الزوجية مطلقاً عند موته، وهي من أسباب الإرث ، ويشترط في الإرث وجود سبب من أسباب الإرث عند موت المورث ، وبهذا احتج الشافعي له فيما قبل انقضاء العدة أيضاً. والفرق لنا : أنَّ العدّة يبقى فيها النكاح في بعض الأحكام ؛ ولهذا لا يحل له نكاح الأخت في عدّة الأخت ونكاحُ الخامسة في عدة الرابعة، فيجوز أن يحكم ببقاء النكاح في العدة في حق الإرث أيضاً، ويردّ ما قصده الزوج من إبطال حقها، بخلاف ما إذا انقضت العدة قبل موته ، فإنّه لا يمكن حينئذ بقاء حكم من أحكام النكاح، فلا يثبت الإرث أيضاً. [١] قوله : وإن ... الخ ؛ قال البِرْجَنْدي ؛ وإن علق بينونتها بشرط ووجـد ذلك الشرط في مرضه ترث إن علّق بفعله، الأحسن أن يقال : إن كان فعله ؛ أي إن كان الشرط فعل الزوج ، سواءً كان فعلاً له منه بد، أو لا، وسواء كان التعليق في الصحة ووجود الفعل في المرض، أو كانا جميعاً فيه ؛ لوجودِ قصد الإبطال فيه، بمباشرة الشرط. أو يفعلها، ولا بد لها منه، قيد في الأخير فقط ؛ أي علق بينونتها بفعلها الضروري، كالصلاة والصوم وكلام الأب وذوي الرحم المحرم، وطلب الحقوق من الخصوم، والأكل والشرب والقيام والقعود والتنفس، ونحو ذلك ففعلت في مرضه ترث ؛ لأنها مضطرة لخوف الهلاك في الدنيا أو العقبى، فكأنه ألجأها إلى المباشرة. أو بغيرهما وقد علق في المرض ؛ أي إذا علّق بينونتها بغير فعل الزوج والزوجة ، بل بفعل أجنبي أو بمجيء الوقت، وقد وقع التعليق والشرط كلاهما في المرض ترث أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ وقوع الطلاق عند وجودِ الشرط إنّما هو بمباشرة التعليق في حال تعلّق حقها بماله، فيكون فاراً، بخلاف ما إذا كان التعليق في الصحة، فإنّها لا ترث ؛ إذ عند التعليق لم يقصد إبطال حقها. [٢) قوله : بينونتها ؛ فيه إشارة إلى أن الفرار إنّما يتحقق في البائن دون الرجعي وإلى أن الواحد والثلاث وما بينهما سواء في الحكم.
In revocable divorce, she inherits in all circumstances. Her inheritance is specified to his death during her iddah. So they (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) responded: "The action is unavoidable for her, so she is compelled to perform it, so her action becomes attributed to the husband as in coercion." By consensus: (And in revocable divorce she inherits in all circumstances) and her inheritance is specified to his death in her iddah: As for when her iddah expires, then he dies, she does not inherit. [1] His saying: 'So they responded' means the response from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and its summary: that his (the husband's) attachment (of the divorce) even if it was in health, but since he attached it to an action unavoidable for her, she is compelled to perform it in every state, so it is as if he caused the nullification of her right. [2] His saying: 'as in coercion' means that if Zayd coerces Amr to destroy another's property, and Amr destroys it, the guarantee is incumbent on Zayd, because the coerced becomes like a tool for the coercer, like a knife for the slaughterer, so the action is transferred to him. [3] His saying: 'in all circumstances' means whether the divorce was at her request or not, and whether the attachment was by her action or his action, and whether the action was avoidable or not; because revocable divorce does not completely remove the marriage, so that intercourse is lawful during the iddah, and he can take her back, so the cause of inheritance remains. This is in al-'Inaya. (4) His saying: 'she does not inherit by consensus' means between us and al-Shafi'i and others, because the marital relationship is absolutely severed at his death, and it is one of the causes of inheritance, and it is required for inheritance that a cause of inheritance exist at the death of the testator; and al-Shafi'i argued with this also regarding before the end of the iddah. Our distinction: that during the iddah the marriage remains in some rulings; therefore it is not permissible for him to marry the sister during the iddah of the sister, nor the fifth during the iddah of the fourth; so it is permissible to judge the continuation of the marriage during the iddah also regarding inheritance, and to nullify the husband's intent to invalidate her right; unlike if the iddah has expired before his death, then it is not possible for any ruling of marriage to remain, so inheritance is not established either. [1] His saying: 'And if... etc.'; al-Birjandi said: And if he attached her separation to a condition and that condition occurred in his illness, she inherits if he attached it to his action. It is better to say: if it was his action; i.e., if the condition was the husband's action, whether the action is avoidable or not, and whether the attachment was in health and the occurrence of the action in illness, or both were in it (illness); because the intent to invalidate exists, by directly performing the condition. Or to her action, and it is unavoidable for her, a condition only in the latter; i.e., he attached her separation to her necessary action, like prayer, fasting, speaking to the father and mahram relatives, demanding rights from opponents, eating, drinking, standing, sitting, breathing, and similar; so she does it in his illness, she inherits; because she is compelled for fear of destruction in this world or the Hereafter, so it is as if he compelled her to do it. Or to something other than the two and he attached in illness; i.e., if he attached her separation to something other than the action of the husband and wife, but to the action of a third party or the arrival of a time, and both attachment and condition occurred in the illness, she also inherits; because the occurrence of divorce upon the existence of the condition is only by the direct attachment in a state where her right is attached to his wealth, so he is a fugitive (from inheritance); unlike if the attachment was in health, then she does not inherit; because at the time of attachment he did not intend to invalidate her right. [2] His saying: 'her separation' contains an indication that evasion (firar) is only realized in a irrevocable divorce, not revocable, and that one, three, and what is between them are equal in the ruling.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, deals with the inheritance rights of a divorced woman in two scenarios: revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) and conditional divorce during the husband's terminal illness (talaq al-marid). The text first establishes the general rule: in revocable divorce, the woman inherits from her ex-husband if he dies during her iddah (waiting period), regardless of the circumstances of the divorce. This is because revocable divorce does not completely dissolve the marriage; some effects, like inheritance, persist during the iddah. The commentary clarifies that this applies whether the divorce was at her request or not, and whether the condition triggering the divorce was action by her or him, avoidable or not. Then it addresses the case where the iddah has expired: she does not inherit, by consensus between the Hanafis and Shafi'is. The Shafi'i school argues that since marriage ends at death, there is no cause for inheritance; the Hanafi response is that during the iddah, the marriage partially persists, so inheritance is valid. The passage then delves into conditional divorce (ta'liq) made by a sick husband intending to deprive his wife of inheritance (firar). The commentary explains several sub-rulings based on the type of condition: if the condition is the husband's own action (whether avoidable or not), she inherits because he directly caused the divorce. If the condition is an action of the wife that is unavoidable (like eating, praying, or breathing), she inherits because she is compelled, and the divorce is attributed to him by analogy to coercion (ikrah). If the condition is neither the husband's nor the wife's action (e.g., an event or a third party's act), and the ta'liq was made during illness, she inherits; if the ta'liq was made in health, she does not. The key principle is that the husband's intention to evade inheritance (firar) is considered, and inheritance rights are protected. The passage also notes that this ruling applies only to irrevocable divorce (bain), not revocable, and that one, three, or any number of divorces are treated equally.

Key Terms
طلاق رجعي (talaq raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take the wife back during the iddah without a new marriage contract.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period after divorce or death, during which a woman cannot remarry and some marriage effects continue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a revocable divorce, if the husband dies after the wife's iddah has expired, does she inherit according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because she was still connected to him.
B) No, because the marriage has ended completely after iddah.
C) Yes, by consensus of all schools.
D) No, only if he died during the iddah.

Key Takeaway
A revocably divorced woman inherits only if her husband dies during her iddah, as the marriage partially continues.
